Division of Conception and Assent into Necessary and Acquired
Author explains the division of conception and assent into necessary and acquired.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish necessary (daruri) from acquired (kasbi) knowledge for both conception and assent, and explain the implicit division.

Core Idea
Both conception and assent can be either self-evident or acquired through reflection.

In this passage, the commentator explains the division of conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) into necessary (daruri) and acquired (kasbi). This division is presented as self-evident, and the commentator highlights that the matn's wording implies the division of each kind of knowledge into these two categories. Understanding this division is foundational for distinguishing between axiomatic premises and those that require proof in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of knowledge
The passage speaks of tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent). Recognize these as the two fundamental categories of knowledge in mantiq.
2
Identify the two attributes
The attributes are darura (necessity) and iktisab (acquisition) or nazar (reflection). Note that 'by necessity' (بالضرورة) indicates the division is self-evident.
3
Understand explicit versus implicit division
The matn explicitly divides the attributes between the two, but by implication each of tasawwur and tasdiq is itself divided into necessary and acquired.
The commentator notes this is more eloquent than explicit mention.
4
Give examples
Provide examples: necessary tasawwur (conception of heat), acquired tasawwur (conception of an angel), necessary tasdiq (assent that the sun is shining), acquired tasdiq (assent that the world is originated).
5
Recognize the self-evident nature
The phrase 'by necessity' indicates this division is badihi, requiring no proof.
This is a foundational axiom in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في مقدمة علم المنطق والتصور و يقتسمان بالضرورة، الضرورة. كالاذعان بأن زيداً قائم او السلبية كالاعتقاد بانه ليس بقائم، فقد اختار المصنف مذهب الحكماء حيث جعل التصديق نفس الاذعان و الحكم دون المجموع المركب منه و من تصور الطرفين كما زعمه الامام الرازي و اختار مذهب القدماء ايضاً حيث جعل متعلق الاذعان والحكم الذي هو الجزء الاخير للقضية هو النسبة الخبرية الثبوتية أو السلبية لا وقوع النسبة الثبوتية التقييدية اولا وقوعها و سیشیر المصنف الى تثليث اجزاء القضية فى مباحث القضايا. قوله « والتصور»: سواء كان ادراكا لامر واحد كتصور زيد، او لامور متعددة بدون نسبة كتصور زيد وعمرو و بكر، أو مع نسبة غير تامة اي التي لا يصح السكوت عليها كتصور غلام زيد او تامة انشائية كتصور اضرب او خبرية مدركة بادراك غير اذعانى كما فى صور التخييل والشك والوهم. سارى قوله «و يقتسمان»: الاقتسام بمعنى القسمة على ما في الاساس اى يقسم التصور و التصديق كلا من وصفى الضرورة اى الحصول بلانظر والاكتساب اى الحصول بالنظر فيأخذ التصور قسماً من الضرورة فيصير ضرورياً و قسماً من الاكتساب فيصير كسبياً و كذا الحال في التصديق فالمذكور فى هذه العبارة صريحاً هو انقسام الضرورة و الاكتساب ويعلم انقسام كل من التصور و التصديق الى الضروري و الاكتسابي ضمناً و كناية وهى ابلغ و احسن من التصريح. قوله :«بالضرورة»: اشارة الى ان هذه القسمة بديهية لا تحتاج الى تجشم. (ه) قوله ويقتسمان اى التصديق والتصور السابقا الذكر وصف الضرورة والنظر فيأخذ كل منها وصفاً من كل منها فتصور ضروری و تصدیق ضروری و تصور نظری و تصدیق نظری ایضاً فالمذكور فى عبارة المتن صريحاً هو انقسام الضرورة والاكتساب بين التصور و التصديق و اذا حاز كل من التصور والتصديق وصفاً من كل من الضرورة والاكتساب فقد انقسم كل من التصور والتصديق انفسهما الى الضروري و النظرى من باب الملازمة البيئة.
In the introduction to the science of logic: conception and assent are divided by necessity. For example, assent that Zayd is standing, or the negative like the belief that he is not standing. The author chose the doctrine of the philosophers, making assent itself the act of mental acceptance and judgment, not the composite consisting of it and the conception of the two terms, as al-Imam al-Razi claimed. And he also chose the doctrine of the ancients, making the object of mental acceptance and judgment, which is the last part of the proposition, the affirmative or negative predicative relation, not the occurrence of the restrictive affirmative relation or its non-occurrence. And the author will indicate the tripartite division of the proposition's parts in the section on propositions. His statement 'and conception': whether it is the perception of a single thing like the conception of Zayd, or of multiple things without relation like the conception of Zayd, Amr, and Bakr, or with an incomplete relation that does not allow pause, like the conception of Zayd's slave, or with a complete constructional relation like the conception of 'hit', or a declarative relation perceived by a non-assenting perception as in cases of imagination, doubt, and illusion. His statement 'and they are divided': the division of conception and assent each to the two attributes of necessity, i.e., attainment without reflection, and acquisition, i.e., attainment by reflection. So conception takes a part from necessity, becoming necessary, and a part from acquisition, becoming acquired; similarly for assent. What is explicitly mentioned in this phrase is the division of necessity and acquisition, and it is known implicitly and by allusion that each of conception and assent is divided into necessary and acquired, and this is more eloquent and better than explicit mention. His statement 'by necessity': an indication that this division is self-evident, not requiring effort. (Marginal note): His statement 'and they are divided' means that the aforementioned assent and conception receive the attributes of necessity and reflection, each taking an attribute from each, so there is necessary conception, necessary assent, theoretical conception, and theoretical assent. So what is explicitly mentioned in the text is the division of necessity and acquisition between conception and assent. When each of conception and assent receives an attribute from each of necessity and acquisition, then each of conception and assent themselves have been divided into necessary and theoretical by way of evident implication.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's statement that conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) are divided by necessity. The commentator begins by giving examples of assent: affirmative like 'Zayd is standing' and negative like 'Zayd is not standing'. He then explains that the author (al-Akhdari or another) adopted the philosophers' view that assent is the act of mental acceptance (idh'an) and judgment itself, not a composite of that act plus the conception of the two terms (as al-Razi held). The author also adopted the ancients' view that the object of the judgment is the predicative relation (affirmative or negative), not the occurrence of a restrictive relation. The commentator then expands on the types of conception: it can be of a single thing (e.g., Zayd), multiple things without relation (e.g., Zayd, Amr, Bakr), with an incomplete relation (e.g., 'Zayd's slave'), or with a complete constructional relation (e.g., 'hit'), or a declarative relation perceived without assent (imagination, doubt, illusion). Next, he explains that the matn's phrase 'they are divided by necessity' means that each (conception and assent) is divided into necessary (daruri) and acquired (kasbi). Necessity means attainment without reflection (nazar); acquisition means attainment by reflection. So conception can be necessary or acquired, and similarly for assent. The commentator notes that the matn explicitly mentions the division of necessity and acquisition, and implicitly indicates the division of conception and assent themselves into necessary and acquired—this is more eloquent. He then says that the phrase 'by necessity' signals that this division is axiomatic (badihi) and does not require proof. The marginal note adds that the matn's wording explicitly divides necessity and acquisition between conception and assent, and by necessary implication, conception and assent are themselves divided into necessary and theoretical. The key point is that knowledge is either self-evident (daruri) or acquired by reflection (kasbi/nazari), and this applies to both tasawwur and tasdiq. Examples: the conception of heat is necessary, the conception of an angel is acquired; the assent that the sun is shining is necessary, the assent that the world is originated is acquired.

Key Terms
التصور
التصديق
الضرورة
الاكتساب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, the matn's phrase 'by necessity' indicates that the division is:
A) required by evidence
B) self-evident
C) based on sense-perception
D) only valid for tasawwur

Key Takeaway
Knowledge (conception and assent) is either self-evident (daruri) or acquired (kasbi/nazari), and this division is axiomatic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether each of the following is necessary or acquired: (a) the conception of 'happiness', (b) the assent that 'God exists'. Explain your reasoning using the definitions from this lesson.
?
Give an original example from usul al-fiqh of a necessary tasawwur and an acquired tasdiq.

The Nature of Assent: Philosophers vs. al-Razi
Author explains the nature of assent and the dispute with al-Razi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the philosophers' view that tasdiq is pure judgment with al-Razi's view that it is a composite including conception of terms.

Core Idea
Assent (tasdiq) is the act of judgment itself, not a composite with conception.

In this passage, the commentator discusses a key disagreement in Islamic logic: what exactly is assent (tasdiq)? The author sides with the philosophers (al-hukama') who hold that tasdiq is the act of mental acceptance (idh'an) and judgment (hukm) alone, not a composite that includes the conception of the two terms of the proposition. Al-Imam al-Razi, on the other hand, considered tasdiq to include the conception of the subject and predicate. This distinction affects how we analyze propositions and knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing views
The author (and philosophers) hold tasdiq = idh'an/hukm alone. Al-Razi holds tasdiq = composite of idh'an/hukm + conception of the two terms.
The passage says: 'the author chose the doctrine of the philosophers, making assent itself the act of mental acceptance and judgment, not the composite'.
2
Understand the implications
If tasdiq includes conception, then assent would be partly conceptual; the line between conception and assent blurs.
This is why the distinction is important for the structure of knowledge.
3
Note the second disagreement
The object of judgment: philosophers say it is the predicative relation (nisba khabariyya), not the occurrence of a restrictive relation.
This relates to the later discussion of proposition parts.
4
Relate to future sections
The commentator hints that the author will discuss the tripartite division of the proposition later.
This shows how the nature of assent connects to propositional analysis.
5
Evaluate the author's choice
The author chose the philosophers' view, which aligns with the ancients (qudama').
This indicates a traditional stance against al-Razi's more complex view.

Distinguishing Simple and Compound Words in Logic
Author defines compound with four conditions and lists four types of simple.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given word as simple (mufrad) or compound (murakkab) by checking the four conditions from the author.

Core Idea
A compound word requires both parts and intended part-for-part indication of meaning.

In the logical tradition, not every word with multiple letters is compound. The author lays out four precise conditions for a word to be compound (murakkab). Any word that fails even one condition is considered simple (mufrad). Understanding these conditions is essential for analyzing definitions and terms in logic and Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word's parts
Check if the word has multiple distinct parts (e.g., syllables or components) in its pronunciation. For example, 'Zayd' has parts (Zay-d), but 'hamza' is a single letter.
2
Check if the meaning has parts
Examine whether the meaning of the word can be divided into components. For instance, the word 'Allah' has a unique meaning without internal components.
3
Verify if parts of the word indicate parts of meaning
Determine if the components of the word each refer to a distinct part of the meaning. In 'al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq' (rational animal), 'ḥayawān' indicates animality and 'nāṭiq' indicates rationality.
4
Confirm intention of indication
Even if parts indicate meaning, the indication must be intended. When 'al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq' is used as a proper name for a person, the indication is not intended; it functions as a simple label.
This is the fourth condition and often overlooked.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في المفرد والمركب واقسامها
قوله « والموضوع» (۳۵) ای : اللفظ الموضوع (٣٦) ان اريد الدلالة بجزء منه على جزء : معناه (۳۷) فهو المركب والا فهو المفرد فالمركب انما يتحقق بتحقق امور اربعة
الاول : ان يكون المفظه جزء . (۳۸)
الثاني: ان يكون المعناه جزء.
الثالث: ان يدل جزء لفظه على جزء معناه . (۳۹)
الرابع: ان يكون هذه الدلالة مرادة (٤٠)
.
فبانتفاء كل من القيود الاربعة يتحقق قسم من المفرد. فالمركب قسم واحد والمفرد اقسام اربعة: الأول: ما لا جزء للفظه نحو همزة الاستفهام . الثاني: ما لا جزء لمعناه نحو لفظ «الله». الثالث: ما لا دلالة الجزء لفظه على جزء معناه نحو «زید» و «عبدالله» علما. الرابع: ما يدل جزء لفظه على جزء معناه لكن هذه الدلالة غير مقصودة (٤١) كالحيوان الناطق (٤٢) علماً للشخص الانساني.
On the simple and compound and their divisions.
His statement 'and the topic': i.e., the coined word. If it is intended that a part of it indicates a part of its meaning, then it is compound; otherwise it is simple. So compound is only realized by the realization of four matters:
First: that its wording has a part.
Second: that its meaning has a part.
Third: that a part of its wording indicates a part of its meaning.
Fourth: that this indication is intended.
So by the absence of each of these four conditions, a type of simple is realized. Thus compound is one type, and simple has four types:
First: what has no part of its wording, like the interrogative hamza.
Second: what has no part of its meaning, like the word 'Allah'.
Third: what has no indication of a part of its wording for a part of its meaning, like 'Zayd' and 'Abdallah' as proper names.
Fourth: what has indication of a part of its wording for a part of its meaning but this indication is not intended, like 'al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq' as a name for the human individual.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage defines the distinction between simple (mufrad) and compound (murakkab) words. The author begins by stating that the topic (al-mawḍūʿ) is the coined word. If it is intended that a part of the word indicates a part of its meaning, then it is compound; otherwise it is simple. Then he lists four conditions for a compound: (1) the word must have parts, (2) the meaning must have parts, (3) the parts of the word must indicate parts of the meaning, and (4) this indication must be intended. If any of these four conditions is missing, the word is simple. Thus there are four types of simple: (1) a word with no parts, e.g., the interrogative hamza; (2) a word whose meaning has no parts, e.g., the name 'Allah'; (3) a word whose parts do not indicate parts of its meaning, e.g., proper names like 'Zayd' or 'Abdallah' (even if they are originally compound, as names they are treated as simple); (4) a word whose parts do indicate parts of its meaning but this indication is not intended when used as a proper name, e.g., 'al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq' (the rational animal) used as a proper name for a human individual. This classification is fundamental to understanding how words signify meanings in logic.

Key Terms
المفرد
المركب
جزء اللفظ
جزء المعنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of the fourth type of simple (where parts indicate meaning but not intended)?
A) The word 'Allah'
B) The interrogative hamza
C) 'Al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq' used as a proper name for a person
D) 'Zayd' as a proper name

Key Takeaway
The classification of words into simple and compound depends on four conditions related to parts and intention of indication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following words as simple or compound using the four conditions: 'بسم الله' (the phrase 'In the name of God') when used as a name for a book; 'الرحمن' (al-Rahman, 'the Most Merciful') as a divine name; 'مكة' (Makkah) as a city name.
?
Given the definition 'الحيوان الناطق' as a definition for human, is it compound or simple? Explain why.

Mafhum, Ma'na, Madlul and the Kulli/Juz'i Division
Defines the concept (mafhum), distinguishes it from meaning (ma'na) and denotation (madlul), and explains the conditions for universality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three aspects of a word (mafhum, ma'na, madlul) and determine whether a concept is kulli or juz'i by stripping accidents of number and convention.

Core Idea
A concept's universality depends on stripping away accidents of number and convention, not on the word's form alone.

In this passage, the author establishes precise terminology for the three facets of a word: what is understood (mafhum), what is intended (ma'na), and what is indicated (madlul). Then he explains how to classify concepts as universal or particular by abstracting from grammatical number and conventional assignments. This parsing exercise is essential for understanding definitions (hadd) and the five universals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three aspects of the word
Find in the Arabic the three phrases describing what is derived from the word: 'mafhum' (by virtue of being understood), 'ma'na' (by virtue of being intended), and 'madlul' (by virtue of being indicated). Note the different angles: understanding, intention, denotation.
These are not separate entities but three perspectives on the same content.
2
Understand the definition of kulli and juz'i
Locate the sentence that defines kulli and juz'i. The key is 'when presented to the mind stripped of accidents'. Notice that the mind's judgment (allowing many or restricting to one) determines the classification.
Accidents include dual, plural, and convention.
3
Recognize the accidents: dual, plural, convention
The passage explicitly lists 'tathniyah' (dual), 'jam'' (plural), and 'tawati'' (convention) as accidents that affect universality. Understand how each forces particularity or can override original universality.
Dual and plural always yield particular concepts. Convention can make a potentially universal word particular or vice versa.
4
Apply the stripping process to examples
Take the examples 'al-Zaydan' (the two Zayds) and 'al-Zaydin' (the Zayds). Strip the dual/plural: the underlying concept 'Zayd' (without number) may be universal, but the dual/plural accident makes it juz'i. Similarly, if convention restricts 'insan' to a particular man, it becomes juz'i.
Always check if the concept can apply to more than one after removing accidents.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: المفهوم. اعلم أن ما استفيد من اللفظ باعتبار أنه يفهم منه يسمى مفهوما، وباعتبار أن هذا المستفاد من اللفظ مقصود منه يسمى معنى، وباعتبار أن اللفظ دال عليه يسمى مدلولا. ثم إن ما يفهم من الشيء عند استعراضه للعقل مجردا عن الطوارئ، إذا جوز العقل صدقه على أمور كثيرة فكلي، وإذا حصره بشخص فجزئي. ومرادنا بقولنا مجردا عن الطوارئ تثنية اللفظ وجمعه، ويفرض له من اتحاد معنى أو كثرته بالتواطئ كالزيدين مثنى والزيدين جمعا، وكالتواطئ على أن إطلاق زيد يفيد صنفا من الناس مثلا، أو أن إطلاق الإنسان يفيد إنسانا بخصوصه. فكلما يفهم من يقال في حقه «المفهوم» في حال أنه من ألفاظ التثنية والجمع يعطى كثرة ولا يقال له كلي، فإن الزيدين تثنية والزيدين جمعا جزئي بالضرورة. والمفهوم الذي توطئ في لفظه تابع لكيفية التواطئ، فإن توطئ على جزئيته فجزئي وإن كان لولا التواطئ يفيد الكلية، وإن توطئ على كليته فكلي وإن كان لولا التواطئ يفيد الجزئية. وعليه فلابد من تقييد المفهوم بكونه مجردا عن طارئ التثنية والجمع والتواطئ.
Section on the concept. Know that what is derived from the word, in terms of being understood from it, is called a mafhum (concept); and in terms of this derived content being intended by it, it is called ma'na (meaning); and in terms of the word indicating it, it is called madlul (denoted object). Then, what is understood from a thing when it is presented to the mind, stripped of accidents: if the mind allows it to apply to many things, it is kulli (universal); and if it restricts it to a single individual, it is juz'i (particular). What we mean by 'stripped of accidents' is the dual and plural forms of the word, and [the concept] is assumed to have either a single meaning or multiplicity by convention, like 'al-Zaydan' (the two Zayds) in the dual and 'al-Zaydin' in the plural, and like the convention that the utterance 'Zayd' indicates a class of people, or that the utterance 'insan' indicates a particular human. Thus, whatever is understood from what is called 'the concept' while it is among the words of dual and plural gives multiplicity and is not called kulli; for 'al-Zaydan' in the dual and 'al-Zaydin' in the plural are necessarily juz'i. The concept that is established in its word is dependent on the manner of convention: if it is established as particular, it is juz'i even though without convention it would indicate universality; and if it is established as universal, it is kulli even though without convention it would indicate particularity. Therefore, it is necessary to restrict the concept to being free from the accident of dual, plural, and convention.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition of the concept (mafhum) and its classification into universal and particular. It first distinguishes three aspects: the concept itself, the intended meaning, and the denoted object. Then, it explains that when the concept is considered abstracted from accidents such as grammatical number (dual/plural) and conventional assignments, if the mind allows it to apply to many, it is universal; if not, particular. The author clarifies that when a word is in dual or plural form, it necessarily denotes a specific number, hence it is particular. Similarly, if by convention a word is assigned to a specific individual, it becomes particular. The key point is that universality and particularity are not intrinsic to the word but depend on the absence of such accidents. This prevents confusion that might arise from assuming that all words are straightforwardly universal.

Key Takeaway
A concept's universality is determined after stripping away accidents like dual, plural, and convention; a word itself is not inherently kulli or juz'i.

The Five Universals

Defining the Universal and its Division by Individual Existence
Author defines 'particular' and 'universal', then divides universals by existence of their individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any universal concept according to the existence status of its individuals using the criterion of mental supposition.

Core Idea
A universal is defined by mental supposition, not external existence; even impossible concepts can be universals.

In the previous lessons, you learned that the five universals are tools for defining and describing. Here, the author refines the concept of 'universal' itself by distinguishing it from 'particular' based on mental supposition (فرض) rather than actual existence. He then introduces a division of universals according to whether their individuals are possible or impossible to exist, and if possible, whether they exist, how many, and with what constraints. This classification is crucial for understanding how logical concepts relate to reality in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Understand the criterion for particular vs. universal
The author states: if a concept can be mentally supposed (فرض) to be true of many, it is particular; otherwise it is universal. Note that 'فرض' here means mental allowance (تجويز العقل), not estimation (تقدير), because one can estimate that a particular applies to many, but that doesn't change its nature.
This criterion is purely conceptual, not dependent on external existence.
2
Identify the two main divisions of universals by individual existence
The author divides universals into those whose individuals are impossible to exist (ممتنع) and those whose individuals are possible (ممكن). The impossible category has no individuals in the external world at all.
Example: the concept of a partner of Allah (شريك الباري) is universal but its individuals are impossible.
3
Subdivide the possible group
Within possible universals, further divisions are based on whether individuals exist, if only one exists with possibility or impossibility of others, or if many exist with finitude or without finitude. The author gives concrete examples for each subcategory.
These subdivisions cover all logical possibilities for the existence of individuals of a universal.
4
Recognize the refutation of the existence requirement
The passage ends by noting that this division refutes those who claim a universal must have actually existing individuals. Impossible universals like شريك الباري show that a concept can be universal even if it has no external instances.
This is a key point for Islamic theology (kalam) where concepts like 'partner of Allah' are used in arguments.

If it is mentally supposed to be true of many, then it is particular; otherwise it is universal. Its individuals are impossible (like the partner of Allah, exalted is His name), or possible but not found (like the phoenix), or only one exists with the possibility of another or its impossibility (like the sun, or the concept of necessary existence), or many with finitude (like the seven planets) or without finitude (like the objects of Allah's knowledge and the rational soul according to the philosophers). His saying 'if it is mentally supposed to be true of many': mental supposition here means the mind's allowance, not estimation; for it is not impossible to estimate a particular as true of many. His saying 'its individuals are impossible': like the partner of Allah. His saying 'or possible': i.e., its individuals are not impossible in the external world, so it includes both the necessary and the contingent particular. His saying 'but not found': like the phoenix. His saying 'with the possibility of another': like the sun. His saying 'or its impossibility': like the concept of necessary existence. His saying 'with finitude': like the seven planets. His saying 'or without it': like the objects of Allah's knowledge and the rational soul according to the philosophers. For the mind, as we have previously mentioned, considers, for example, the concept of the partner of Allah and does not find in this concept that it conceives anything that specifies, restricts, or individualizes it such that it judges it to be particular; rather it finds it a universal concept, and therefore judges it to be universal. (Taqrib, pp. 24-25) And in this division there is a warning to refute the claim of some that the universal must have its individuals existing.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage defines 'particular' and 'universal' and then divides universals based on the existence status of their individuals. The author begins by stating the criterion: if a concept can be mentally supposed to apply to many instances, it is particular; otherwise it is universal. The term 'فرض' (mental supposition) is clarified to mean the mind's allowance (تجويز العقل) rather than actual estimation, because one could estimate a particular as applying to many, but that does not make it universal. The universal is then divided into types according to whether its individuals are impossible to exist (ممتنع), possible but not existent, existing as a single individual with the possibility or impossibility of others, or existing as many with or without finitude. Examples are given for each: the concept of a partner of Allah (impossible), the phoenix (possible but not found), the sun (single with possibility of others), the concept of necessary existence (single with impossibility of others), the seven planets (many with finitude), and the objects of Allah's knowledge or the rational soul (many without finitude, according to philosophers). The commentary then expands on the rationale: the mind considers a concept like 'partner of Allah' and finds it unrestricted, so it judges it universal. This division refutes those who think a universal must have actually existing individuals.

The Negations of Absolute General and Particular are Reversed
Author proves that the negations of a'amm and akhass mutlaq are in the same relation but reversed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that the negation of the more general universal is more specific and vice versa, and test it on examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The negations of a'amm and akhass mutlaq swap their relation.

In the study of the five universals, the relation between a more general and a more specific term is clear. But what happens when we negate both? Intuitively, one might think the same relation holds. This passage from the commentary shows that, in fact, the relation reverses. Understanding this principle sharpens your ability to reason about contradictory pairs in Islamic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the a'amm and akhass mutlaq
Find the two terms in the example: one absolutely general (e.g., 'animal') and one absolutely specific (e.g., 'human'). Verify that every human is an animal but not every animal is human.
2
State the rule about their negations
The negation of the general is more specific than the negation of the specific. That is, 'non-animal' is more specific than 'non-human' (since every non-animal is non-human, but not vice versa).
3
Prove the first direction
Assume the negation of the general is true of something but the negation of the specific is false. Then the specific original must be true, leading to a contradiction. This shows that whenever the negation of the general is true, the negation of the specific must also be true.
This uses the impossibility of both negations being false.
4
Prove the converse does not hold
If the negation of the specific were always true when the negation of the general is true, then the two negations would be equal. But the originals are not equal, so this is absurd. Hence the relation is reversed, not identical.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Choose two terms from Islamic sciences, e.g., 'creature' (a'amm) and 'human' (akhass). Verify that 'non-creature' is more specific than 'non-human'. Explain using the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و نقيضا هما بالعكس و الا فمن وجه و بين نقيضيهما تباين جزئى ( ) :ای كلما صدق عليه احد النقيضين (۲۲) صدق عليه النقيض الاخر اذلو صدق احدهما بدون الاخر لصدق مع عين الآخر ضرورة استحالة ارتفاع النقيضين (۲۳) فيصدق عين الآخر بدون عين الأول لامتناع اجتماع النقيضين و هذا (٢٤) يرفع التساوى بين العينين. مثلاً لو صدق اللاانسان على شيء (۲۵) و لم يصدق عليه اللاناطق لصدق عليه الناطق فيصدق الناطق عليه هيهنا بدون الانسان هذا خلف . (٢٦) قوله» و» نقيضا هما بالعكس» اى نقيضا الاعم والاخص مطلقا اعم و اخص مطلقا لكن بعكس العينين فنقيض الاعم اخص ونقيض الاخص اعم بمعني: ان كلّما صدق عليه نقيض الاعم صدق عليه نقيض الاخص وليس كلما صدق عليه نقيض الاعم. بدون نقيض الاخص صدق عليه نقیض اما الأول (۲۷) فلانه لو صدق نقيض الاعم على : الاخص (۲۸) نصدق مع عين الاخص فيصدق عين الاخص بدون عين خلف (۲۹) هذا ويمتنع مثلاً لو صدق اللاحيوان على شيء بدون اللاانسان لصدق عليه الانسان هناك صدق الحيوان الاستحالة اجتماع النقيضين (۳۰) فيصدق الانسان بدون الحيوان. و أما الثاني: (۳۱) فلانه بعد ما ثبت ان كل نقيض الاعم نقيض الاخص (۳۲) لو كان كل نقيض الاخص نقيض الاعم لكان النقيضان متساويين فيكون نقيضا هما لما العينان متساويين كمامر (۳۳) وقد كان العينان اعم واخص مطلقا هذا خلف
And their two negations are in the reverse, otherwise [the relation is] from one aspect, and between their two negations is partial contrariety. (That is: whenever one of the two negations is true of something, the other negation is true of it, because if one were true without the other, then [the original term] would be true with the other [negation] due to the impossibility of both negations being false, so the other [original term] would be true without the first [original term] due to the impossibility of both negations being true, and this would remove the equality between the two originals. For example: if 'non-human' were true of something and 'non-rational' were not true of it, then 'rational' would be true of it, so 'rational' would be true here without 'human' — this is absurd.) His statement: "and their two negations are in the reverse" means that the two negations of the absolutely general and the absolutely particular are absolutely general and absolutely particular, but reversed relative to the two originals: the negation of the general is particular and the negation of the particular is general, meaning: whenever the negation of the general is true of something, the negation of the particular is true of it, but it is not the case that whenever the negation of the general is true [something] without the negation of the particular, the negation [of the particular] is true of it. As for the first [part]: because if the negation of the general were true of the particular, [then] it would be true together with the particular original, so the particular original would be true without the general original — this is absurd and impossible. For example: if 'non-animal' were true of something without 'non-human', then 'human' would be true of it, so 'animal' would be true [of it] due to the impossibility of both negations being true, thus 'human' would be true without 'animal'. As for the second [part]: because after it was established that every negation of the general is a negation of the particular, if every negation of the particular were a negation of the general, then the two negations would be equal, so they would be negations of two equal originals, as passed, but the two originals were absolutely general and particular — this is absurd.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the rule regarding the relation between the negations of two universals that are in an absolute general-particular relation (a'amm wa akhass mutlaq). The main claim is that the negations are also in the same relation but reversed: the negation of the more general is more specific, and the negation of the more specific is more general. The commentator first gives the general principle: if two negations are such that whenever one is true the other is also true, then they are equal. But here they are not equal. He then illustrates with an example: if 'non-human' is true of something but 'non-rational' is not, then 'rational' must be true, leading to a contradiction because 'rational' would be true without 'human'. This shows that the negation of the more general (e.g., non-human) is more general than the negation of the more specific (e.g., non-rational). The proof has two parts: first, showing that whenever the negation of the general is true, the negation of the specific is also true (by assuming the contrary leads to an absurdity); second, showing that the converse does not hold, because if it did, the two negations would be equal, contradicting the original inequality of the originals. The example uses 'animal' and 'human', showing that 'non-animal' (negation of the general) is more specific than 'non-human' (negation of the specific). In Islamic terms, consider 'creature' (a'amm) and 'human' (akhass). The negation of 'creature' is 'non-creature', which is more specific than 'non-human', because everything that is non-creature is non-human, but not vice versa.

اخص مطلق

Key Takeaway
When two universals are in an absolute general-particular relation, their negations are in the same relation but reversed.

Partial Contrariety and Negations of Terms with Generality from One Aspect
Author defines partial contrariety and explains its application to negations of terms related by 'umum min wajh'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the relation between the negations of two universals as partial contrariety, complete contrariety, or generality from one aspect.

Core Idea
Partial contrariety encompasses both complete contrariety and generality from one aspect.

In the relations of universals, we have seen equality, generality-particularity, and complete contrariety. This passage introduces partial contrariety as an overarching category for relations where each universal is sometimes true without the other. Understanding this clarifies the relation between negations, which can vary between complete contrariety and 'min wajh' depending on the terms. This is essential for accurate logical reasoning in Islamic sciences like fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two universals
Look for the two universals whose relation is being discussed. They could be original terms or their negations.
The passage often contrasts original terms with their negations.
2
Determine the original relation
Check if the two are always true together (equality), one always true with the other (generality-particularity), never true together (contrariety), or sometimes both true and sometimes not (generality from one aspect).
The passage focuses on the last two: complete contrariety (tabayun kulli) and generality from one aspect (umum min wajh).
3
Apply the definition of partial contrariety
Partial contrariety means each universal is true without the other in some cases. This holds whether they never intersect (complete contrariety) or intersect partially (generality from one aspect).
This definition is key to analyzing negations.
4
Examine the negations
For the negations of the two universals, check if they are never true together (complete contrariety) or sometimes together (generality from one aspect). Their relation is always partial contrariety.
For example, the negations of 'animal' and 'white' have generality from one aspect; the negations of 'animal' and 'non-human' have complete contrariety.

His statement: "and otherwise [the relation is] from one aspect" means: if they are not completely coincident from both sides nor from one side at all, then [the relation is] from one aspect. His statement: "partial contrariety" — partial contrariety is that each of the two universals is true of something without [the other]... (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (h) His statement: "and between their two negations is partial contrariety" — it means that each of the two concepts is true without the other in some cases, whether they are never true together at all, like the two completely contrary terms, or they are true together in some instances and not true together in others, like generality from one aspect. So partial contrariety encompasses complete contrariety and generality from one aspect, because it does not require subsumption under each of them; therefore the author did not mention it in the relations of universals. (Page 29) 34 The gloss: like the two completely contrary terms. And they [the two negations] are such that each is true without the other in some cases. For if they are true together as well, then between them is generality and particularity from one aspect; if they are never true together at all, then between them is complete contrariety. So partial contrariety is realized within generality and particularity from one aspect and also within complete contrariety. Then, the two things between which there is generality from one aspect: sometimes between their negations there is also generality from one aspect, like 'animal' and 'white', for between their negations, which are 'non-animal' and 'non-white', there is also generality from one aspect; and sometimes between their negations there is complete contrariety, like 'animal' and 'non-human', for between them there is generality from one aspect, but between their negations, which are 'non-animal' and 'human', there is complete contrariety. Therefore they said: between the negations of the more general and more specific from one aspect there is partial contrariety, not only generality and particularity from one aspect nor only complete contrariety. His statement: "like the two completely contrary terms" — just as between the negations of the general and particular from one aspect there is partial contrariety, similarly between the negations of two completely contrary terms there is partial contrariety. For if each of the two originals is sometimes true with the negation of the other, then each of the two negations is true with the other original, so each of the two negations is true without the other in some cases — and that is partial contrariety. Then, it may be realized within complete contrariety, like 'existent' and 'non-existent', for between their negations, which are 'non-existent' and 'non-non-existent', there is also complete contrariety; and it may be realized within generality and particularity from one aspect, like 'human' and 'stone', for between their negations, which are 'non-human' and 'non-stone', there is generality from one aspect. Therefore they said that between their negations there is partial contrariety, so that it is valid for all these cases. Know also: the author deferred mentioning the negations of the two completely contrary terms for two reasons: first, the aim of brevity by analogy with the negations of the general and particular from one aspect; second, because the conception of partial contrariety in itself, abstracted from its particular instances, is dependent on the conception of its two instances, which are generality from one aspect and complete contrariety. So before mentioning both of its instances... (5) His statement: "like the two completely contrary terms" means that the relation between the negations of the general from one aspect, which is partial contrariety, is like the relation between the negations of the two completely contrary terms. And it is not hidden in this analogy that it imports the known onto the unknown, because the explanation of the relation between the negations of the two completely contrary terms had not preceded so that the relation between the negations of the general and particular from one aspect could be analogized to it. But the motive of brevity led him to commit this problematic thing. (al-Taqrib, p. 28)
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage defines 'partial contrariety' (tabayun juz'i) and clarifies its application to the negations of universals that are related by 'generality from one aspect' (umum min wajh). The commentator explains that when the original two universals are not fully coincident nor one-sided, the relation is 'from one aspect'. Partial contrariety means each universal is sometimes true without the other. This definition covers both extremes: if they are never true together, it is complete contrariety; if they are sometimes together and sometimes not, it is generality from one aspect. Thus partial contrariety is a broader category that includes both. The commentator then applies this to negations: for two terms like 'animal' and 'white' (general from one aspect), their negations 'non-animal' and 'non-white' also have generality from one aspect. For terms like 'animal' and 'non-human', their negations 'non-animal' and 'human' have complete contrariety. So the relation between the negations is not fixed; it can be either, hence it is described as partial contrariety. The commentator also discusses why the author deferred discussing the negations of completely contrary terms: for brevity by analogy and because the concept of partial contrariety depends on understanding its two instances (generality from one aspect and complete contrariety). The gloss notes the weakness of the analogy since the relation of negations of contrary terms was not yet explained, but it is tolerated for brevity. In Islamic terms, consider 'wajib' (obligatory) and 'haram' (forbidden) — these are completely contrary? Actually, they are sometimes considered contradictory in a sense, but the example 'existent' and 'non-existent' illustrates complete contrariety, while 'human' and 'stone' illustrates generality from one aspect (some things are both non-human and non-stone, like a tree).

Key Terms
تباين جزئى (tabayun juz'i): A relation between two universals where each is true without the other in some instances; encompasses both complete contrariety and generality from one aspect.
تباين كلى (tabayun kulli): Complete contrariety: the two universals are never true together and never false together (e.g., existent and non-existent).
عموم من وجه (umum min wajh): Generality from one aspect: both universals have some instances in common, but each also has instances not shared by the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relation between the negations of two universals that have a 'umum min wajh' relation?
A) Always 'umum min wajh
B) Always tabayun kulli
C) Tabayun juz'i
D) Always equality

Key Takeaway
Partial contrariety is the broad relation that includes both complete contrariety and generality from one aspect, and it is the universal relation between the negations of terms that are in a 'min wajh' relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identify the relation between the negations of 'wajib' (obligatory) and 'haram' (forbidden) using the definitions of partial contrariety, complete contrariety, and generality from one aspect. First determine the relation between the originals, then the negations.
?
Construct an example from Islamic law where two legal categories (e.g., 'salah' and 'sawm') have a 'umum min wajh' relation, then state the relation between their negations and justify why it is partial contrariety.

The Two Senses of 'Particular' in Islamic Logic
Author defines the particular in two senses and begins the order of the five universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish true particular from relative particular and apply the distinction to concepts in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The same term 'particular' can mean absolute uniqueness or relative specificity.

In Islamic logic, the term 'particular' (juz'i) has two distinct meanings. Understanding this dual usage is critical because it affects how we classify concepts in definitions and arguments. This lesson will clarify both senses with examples from the tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Look at the opening line: 'وقد يقال الجزئى للاخص و هو اعم'. It tells us that 'particular' can be said of the more specific (akhass), and that it is more general (a'am) than the true particular. This introduces the relative particular (al-juz'i al-idafi).
The 'true particular' is the one that cannot be predicated of more than one individual.
2
Contrast with examples
True particular example: Zayd (a specific person). Relative particular example: 'human' is particular compared to 'animal', even though 'human' is universal when compared to Zayd. Note the shift in scope.
3
Test with Islamic science examples
Apply the distinction: In kalam, the concept 'the world' is a relative particular compared to 'everything temporally originated', but a true particular cannot be predicated of many. Try classifying 'the Prophet Muhammad', 'Makkan', 'angel Gabriel'.
This tool is used to avoid confusion when defining terms in fiqh and kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الكليات الخمس وقد يقال الجزئى للاخص و هو اعم. والكليات خمس ؛ الاول: الجنس () و هو لا يتأتى ذكره. قوله « والكليات» اى الكليات التي لها افراد بحسب نفس الامر في قول المصنف الاول الجنس اعلم انه جرت عادتهم على تقديم الجنس على بواقى الكليات ثم تقديم النوع على الثلاثة الباقية ثم تقديم الفصل على الاخير بن ثم تقديم الخاصة على العرض العام. اما تقديم الجنس على النوع فلكونه جزء منه والجزء مقدم على الكل بالطبع فقدم بالوضع ايضاً ليتوافقا و اما على الفصل، فلكونه اعم منه فهو اشهر واحلى والاجلى يقدم على الاخفى ولذا يقدم عليه في الحد التام ايضاً كما سيأتي و اما على الخاصة والعرض العام فلكونه جزء الماهية وكونهما خارجين عنها واما تقديم النوع على الفصل فلانه عين حقيقة الافراد و هو جزئها والكل مقدم على الجزء و لذا قدمه الكاتبي على الجنس ايضاً. فان قيل : هذا ينا في ماسبق في وجه تقديم الجنس على النوع كما هو ظاهر فكيف التوفيق ؟
On the five universals. And it is said that the particular is the more specific, and it is more general. The universals are five: first: genus, and it is not possible to mention it. His statement: 'And the universals' means the universals that have individuals in accordance with the actual state of affairs. On the author's statement: 'first: genus', know that their custom is to give precedence to genus over the remaining universals, then to give precedence to species over the three remaining, then to give precedence to differentia over the last, then to give precedence to property over accidental general. As for giving precedence to genus over species, it is because it is a part of it, and the part is prior to the whole by nature, so it is given precedence in arrangement as well for harmony. And as for (precedence) over differentia, it is because it is more general than it, so it is more well-known and clearer, and the clearer is given precedence over the less clear, and for this reason it is given precedence over it in the complete definition as well, as will come. And as for (precedence) over property and accidental general, it is because it is part of the essence while they are outside it. And as for giving precedence to species over differentia, it is because it is the very reality of the individuals and it is their part, and the whole is prior to the part, and for this reason al-Katibi gave it precedence over genus as well. If it is said: This contradicts what came before regarding the reason for giving precedence to genus over species, as is apparent, then how is reconciliation?
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the beginning of the section on the five universals (al-kulliyat al-khams). It first clarifies the term 'particular' (juz'i), noting that it is used in two senses: one is the particular that cannot be predicated of many (true particular, haqiqi), and the other is the more specific (al-akhass) of something, which is relative particular (idafi). The relative particular is more general than the true particular because everything that is a true particular is also a relative particular under some universal concept, but not vice versa. The author then states the five universals: genus (jins), species (naw'), differentia (fasl), property (khaṣṣa), and accidental general (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm). The commentary explains why genus is presented first. The reasons include: (1) Genus is a part of species, and the part is naturally prior to the whole, so it is placed first for harmony. (2) Genus is more general than differentia, hence clearer and better known, so it is placed first in definition as well. (3) Genus is part of the essence (mahiyya), while property and accidental general are outside the essence, so genus has priority. The passage then explains why species is presented before differentia: species is the entire reality of the individuals and is the whole relative to the differentia which is a part; the whole is prior to the part. It even notes that al-Katibi gave species precedence over genus, which seems contradictory. The passage ends with an objection: this reasoning contradicts the earlier reason for giving genus precedence over species, and asks how to reconcile. The examples are from the Islamic tradition: genus (like 'animal'), species (like 'human'), differentia (like 'rational'), property (like 'laughing'), accidental general (like 'white').

Key Terms
الجزئي الحقيقي
الجزئي الإضافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Human
B) Zayd
C) Animal
D) Rational

Key Takeaway
The term 'particular' has two meanings: true (unique individual) and relative (more specific than something else), and this distinction is essential for clear classification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each as true particular, relative particular, or universal: 'the Quran', 'a revealed book', 'the second caliph Umar', 'human', 'prophet'. Explain your reasoning.
?

Why Genus Comes First: The Logical Order of the Five Universals
Author defines the particular in two senses and explains the order of presentation of the five universals, starting with genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the reasons for the standard order of the five universals (genus, species, differentia, property, accident) and address an apparent contradiction.

Core Idea
Genus is presented first because it is the most general and foundational universal.

The five universals are always taught in a specific order: genus, species, differentia, property, accidental general. This order is not arbitrary; it reflects deep logical priorities based on part-whole relationships, generality, and essence. In this lesson, we unpack the reasoning behind each step and confront an objection raised by al-Katibi's alternative ordering.

Guided Path
1
List the standard order
From the passage: 'they give precedence to genus over the remaining universals, then to species over the three remaining, then to differentia over the last, then to property over accidental general.' Write this order down.
The order: 1. Genus, 2. Species, 3. Differentia, 4. Property, 5. Accident.
2
Identify the reasons for genus's priority
Genus is given precedence over species because it is a part of species (part is prior to whole). Over differentia because it is more general and clear. Over property and accident because it is part of the essence while they are outside. These are three distinct arguments.
The passage also notes genus is placed first in complete definitions (hadd)
3
Understand species priority over differentia
Species is the whole reality of individuals; differentia is a part. The whole is prior to the part, so species precedes differentia. This might seem to conflict with genus priority, but the passage resolves it.
Al-Katibi even gave species precedence over genus, which shows the complexity.
4
Resolve the objection
The passage ends with an objection: 'This contradicts what came before regarding the reason for giving precedence to genus over species.' The resolution is implied: the order is based on different considerations at each step; genus is prior as a part, species is prior as a whole relative to differentia. Which one is 'more prior' depends on context.
The commentator leaves the reconciliation as an exercise, but the logical structure shows no real contradiction.

The Five Universals and Why 'Kulli' is Essential
Author defines the five universals and defends including 'kulli'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the five universals by their definitions and evaluate why the term 'kulli' is necessary in their definition.

Core Idea
Without 'kulli', the definitions of universals would apply to their own definitions—absurd!

This passage delves into the precise definitions of the five universals and a crucial debate: does the definition of each universal need to include the word 'kulli'? The author argues that without it, the definitions would inadvertently apply to the definitions (hudud) of species and genera themselves, leading to logical absurdities. We'll walk through the definitions and the refutation of al-Razi's objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base definition
The passage starts with 'al-maqul 'ala al-kathra...' which is the common definition of the five universals. Understand that this definition is being refined.
This is the starting point for the classification.
2
Distinguish near vs distant genus
The author explains that genus can be near (qarib) like 'animal' for man, or distant (ba'id) like 'body'. Understand why this matters.
Near genus answers the question for all participants; distant does not.
3
Enumerate the five universals
From the analysis of the universal in relation to its individuals, the author derives genus, species, differentia, property, general accident. Confirm you can list them with their definitions.
These are the five: jins, naw', fasl, khassa, 'arad 'amm.
4
Understand al-Razi's objection
Al-Razi claimed that 'kulli' is redundant because 'predicate upon many' already means universal. Grasp the objection.
This sets up the author's refutation.
5
Follow the author's refutation
The author shows that without 'kulli', the definition of species would also apply to 'rational animal' (the definition of man), which is absurd. Thus 'kulli' is necessary.
This is a key logical move to prevent self-reference.

The predicate upon the many differing in realities in answer to 'what is it' (mahuwa). If the answer regarding the essence and regarding some participants is the answer regarding it and regarding the whole, then it is near, like 'animal'. In the mind or externally, [the universals] are confined to five types. As for the hypothetical universals that have no instantiation externally or mentally, no serious purpose attaches to investigating them. Then, when the universal is related to its individuals realized in actual existence, it is either the very reality of those individuals – that is species – or a part of their reality. If it is the complete commonality between some of them and some others, it is genus; otherwise it is differentia. These three are called essentials (dhatiyyat). Or it is external to them and is called accident ('arad). Then it either is specific to individuals of one reality or not. The first is special property (khassa) and the second is general accident ('arad 'amm). This is the proof of the confinement of the universals to five. His statement: 'al-maqul' means the predicate. His statement: 'in answer to mahuwa' – mahuwa is the question about the complete reality. So if the question is limited to mentioning one thing, then the question is about the complete essence specific to it, so species occurs in the answer if the mentioned thing is particular, or the complete definition (hadd tamm) if the mentioned thing is a universal reality. And if many things are combined in the question, then the question is about the complete common essence among those things. Then if those things are agreeing in reality, what is asked about is the complete unified reality agreed upon among those things, so species occurs in the answer. If they are differing in reality, what is asked about is the complete common reality among those differing realities. And you have known that the complete essential common property among differing realities is genus, so genus occurs in the answer. So genus: it is necessary that... [text unclear]. And if that answer is regarding the essence and regarding each of the differing essences that share with it in that genus, then the genus is near (qarib) like 'animal' where it occurs as an answer to the question about man and about everything that shares with it in animality. And if it does not occur as an answer regarding the essence and regarding everything that shares with it in that genus, then it is distant (ba'id) like 'body' where it occurs as an answer to the question about man, stone, and horse, but does not occur as an answer to the question about man, tree, and horse, for example. Rather, [it occurs] in answer to 'what thing' as will come, and the last does not occur in the answer at all. And it appears from some that its omission is to prevent redundancy, for 'predicate upon many' suffices due to being synonymous, except that its indication is detailed while the indication of 'the universal' is summary. For the universal, as mentioned, is a concept that does not prevent assuming its truth about many – i.e., it is eligible by mere conception to be predicated of them. This is what is meant by 'predicate upon many' as is not hidden. Hence Imam al-Razi objected to al-Shaykh [Ibn Sina] that the addition of the word 'al-kulli' is unnecessary because it is synonymous with 'predicate upon many'. And likewise some investigators in the commentary on the treatise said that the word 'al-kulli' is redundant and 'predicate upon many' is the genus for the five, and 'in many' excludes the particular because it is predicated of one, e.g., 'this is Zayd'. I say: the truth is that the word 'al-kulli' is necessary in the definition of the universals, otherwise their definitions (rusum) would not be comprehensive, because they would apply to the definitions (hudud) of the species, genera, differentiae, etc. For just as 'man' is said to be 'predicate upon many agreeing in reality in answer to what is it', so too its definition – i.e., 'rational animal' – is said [the same]. And similarly for the others. Thus if the word 'al-kulli' were not mentioned in the definitions of the universals, it would follow that the definitions (hudud) of species would be species, the definitions of genera would be genera, and so on, which is not the case.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) on the definition of the five universals. The author begins by quoting the standard definition: 'al-maqul 'ala al-kathra al-mukhtalifa al-haqa'iq fi jawab mahuwa' — that which is predicated of many differing realities in answer to the question 'what is it'. He then explains that if the answer covers the essence and all its participants, the genus is 'near' (qarib), like 'animal' for man; otherwise it is 'distant' (ba'id), like 'body' for man and stone. Next, he enumerates the five universals: genus (jins), species (naw'), differentia (fasl), special property (khassa), and general accident ('arad 'amm). They are derived from analyzing the universal in relation to its individuals: if it is the very reality, it is species; if a part of that reality, it is either genus (if the complete commonality) or differentia (if not); if external to the reality, it is either specific (khassa) or general ('arad 'amm). This division is exhaustive. The author then turns to a controversy: some scholars, including Imam al-Razi, argued that the word 'kulli' (universal) is redundant because 'predicate upon many' already conveys the same meaning, and thus the definition of the five universals should omit 'kulli'. The author refutes this by showing that without 'kulli', the definitions (rusum) of the universals would incorrectly apply to the definitions (hudud) of species, genera, etc. For example, the definition of man — 'rational animal' — would itself satisfy the definition of species if 'kulli' were omitted, which is absurd. Hence, 'kulli' is essential to ensure that the definitions of the universals do not inadvertently include the definitions of the things being classified.

D) To make the definition clearer for beginners

Key Takeaway
The inclusion of 'kulli' in the definitions of the five universals is essential to prevent the definitions from applying to their own definitions, a key refinement in logical classification.

Proximate vs Remote Genus in Definitions
Author distinguishes proximate and remote genus by their scope in answering essence questions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given genus is proximate or remote by applying the criterion of whether it answers for all species sharing that genus.

Core Idea
Proximate genus covers all species; remote genus misses some.

This passage refines our understanding of genus by dividing it into proximate and remote. When we ask 'what is it?' about multiple things, the answer is a genus only if the things have different essences. But not all genera answer equally: some answer for every species under them, others do not. Mastering this distinction helps you evaluate definitions precisely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question scenario
Notice the author sets up two cases: a question about one thing, or about multiple things. Focus on the multiple-things case, because that's where genus appears.
If multiple things share the same reality, the answer is species (naw'), not genus.
2
Check if the realities are identical or different
If the things differ in essence (haqiqa), the common element between them is genus. If they are identical, the answer is species.
The genus is the 'complete essential reality common among different realities.'
3
Extract the genus from the answer
The genus appears in the answer when the question asks about things with different essences. For example, 'animal' answers for human and horse because both differ in essence but share animateness.
The genus must be predicated of each different reality.
4
Test if the genus answers for every relevant species
Ask: does this genus answer 'what is it?' for the essence in question and for every other species that shares this genus? If yes, it is proximate (qarib). If no, it is remote (ba'id).
Remote genus answers for some but not all, e.g., 'body' for human and stone but not for tree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال على ذكر امر واحد كان السؤال عن تمام الماهية المختصة به فيقع النوع في الجواب ان كان المذكور امراً شخصياً، او الحد التام ان كان المذكور حقيقة كلية و ان جمع فى السؤال بين امور كان السؤال عن تمام الماهية المشتركة بين تلك الأمور، ثم تلك الامور ان كانت متفقة الحقيقة كان المسئول عنه تمام الحقيقة المتفقة المتحدة في تلك الأمور، فيقع النوع ايضاً في الجواب و ان كانت مختلفة الحقيقة كان المسئول عنه تمام الحقيقة المشتركة بين تلك الحقايق المختلفة . وقد عرفت ان التمام الذاتى المشترك بين الحقايق المختلفة هو الجنس - فيقع الجنس فى الجواب فالجنس لا بد ان يقع جواباً عن الماهية و عن بعض الحقايق المخالفة لها المشاركة اياها في ذلك الجنس فان كان ذلك جواباً عن الماهية و عن كل من الماهيات المختلفة المشاركة لها في ذلك الجنس فالجنس قريب كالحيوان حيث يقع جواباً للسؤال عن الانسان و عن كل ما يشاركه فى الماهية الحيوانية و ان لم يقع جواباً عن الماهية وعن كل ما يشاركها في ذلك الجنس فبعيد كالجسم حيث يقع جواباً عن السؤال بالانسان والحجر و الفرس ولا يقع جواباً عن السؤال بالانسان و الشجر و الفرس مثلاً بل في جواب اى شيء كما سيأتى والاخير لا يقع في الجواب اصلاً.
If the question mentions one thing, the question is about the complete essence specific to it, so the species (naw') is given in the answer if the mentioned thing is individual, or the complete definition (hadd tamm) if it is a universal reality. And if the question combines things, it is about the complete essence common among those things. Then if those things are of identical reality, the answer is about the complete unified reality, so the species is also given in the answer. And if they are of different realities, the answer is about the complete reality common among those different realities. You have known that the complete essential reality common among different realities is the genus, so genus occurs in the answer. The genus must occur as an answer about the essence and about some realities that differ from it but share that genus. If it is an answer about the essence and about every different reality sharing that genus, then the genus is proximate (qarib), like 'animal' when it occurs as an answer to the question about human and about everything that shares animality in essence. But if it is not an answer about the essence and about everything that shares that genus, then it is remote (ba'id), like 'body' when it occurs as an answer to the question about human, stone, and horse, but does not occur as an answer to the question about human, tree, and horse, for example. Rather it occurs in answer to 'what thing?' as will come, and the last does not occur in the answer at all.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage explains how genus (الجنس) functions in answering questions about essence (ماهية). It begins by setting up two scenarios: if the question is about a single thing, the answer is either the species (if individual) or the complete definition (if universal). But if the question combines multiple things, the answer depends on whether those things share the same reality or not. If they share the same reality, the answer is the species (النوع) describing that unified essence. If they have different realities, the answer must refer to the common essential element among them, which is the genus. The author then defines two types of genus: proximate (قريب) and remote (بعيد). Proximate genus answers for the specific essence and for all other species that fall under that same genus. For example, 'animal' answers 'what is it?' for human and also for horse, because both share the genus 'animal'. Remote genus, however, answers for the essence but not for all species under that genus. For example, 'body' answers for human and stone and horse, but does not answer for human, tree, and horse, because tree is not under the genus 'body'? Actually 'body' is remote genus for human and stone, but not for tree. The passage emphasizes that remote genus is still a genus but with narrower scope. The last phrase indicates that there is a further distinction: 'the last does not occur in the answer at all'—likely referring to something else, perhaps a non-genus. This distinction is crucial for understanding how logicians classify answers in definitions and how genus is used in the five universals.

Key Terms
جنس
جنس قريب
جنس بعيد

Key Takeaway
Proximate genus covers all species under it; remote genus leaves some out, affecting the precision of definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Necessity of 'Kulli' in Defining the Five Universals
Defense of the necessity of 'kulli' in the definitions of the five universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'kulli' and 'al-maqul 'ala al-kathra' and explain why the inclusion of 'kulli' is necessary to prevent the definitions of the five universals from applying to their own limits.

Core Idea
Without 'kulli', the definitions of universals would include their limits, causing logical circularity.

In this passage, the author defends the traditional inclusion of the word 'kulli' in the definitions of the five universals against objections that it is synonymous with 'al-maqul 'ala al-kathra' and thus redundant. He argues that the two terms are not identical: 'kulli' carries a condition of multiple individuals that 'al-maqul 'ala al-kathra' lacks, and omitting it leads to the definitions applying to the limits of the universals themselves. This lesson walks through the author's argument and its logical significance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
Notice that some scholars, including Imam al-Razi, claim that 'kulli' is redundant because 'al-maqul 'ala al-kathra' conveys the same meaning. They argue that the phrase 'al-maqul 'ala al-kathra' suffices as the genus for the five universals and excludes particulars.
2
Understand the Author's Distinction
The author explains that 'kulli' refers to a concept that does not prevent assuming its truth of many individuals by mere conception, whereas 'al-maqul 'ala al-kathra' implies actual predication in answer to 'what is it?'. The former explicitly requires the possibility of multiple individuals, while the latter does not necessarily.
3
Trace the Consequence of Omission
If 'kulli' is omitted, the definition 'al-maqul 'ala al-kathra' would apply not only to universals like 'man' but also to their limits, such as 'animal rational'. This would make those limits species, genera, etc., which is not the case.
This is the core logical argument of the passage.
4
Assess the Counterargument
Consider the possible objection that the limits of universals are themselves universals. The author argues that this would break the necessary contrast between a universal and its limit, and it would be an arbitrary departure from the established position.
5
Evaluate the Linguistic Refinement
The author also criticizes the phrase 'al-maqul 'ala al-kathra' for linguistic reasons (e.g., 'kathra' should imply at least three, and the plural form is for rational beings), suggesting a better alternative from earlier in the text.
This shows the intersection of logic and grammar in the Islamic tradition.
