و يظهر من بعضهم ان حذفه لدفع الاستدراك ، فان المقول على الكثرة يغنى عنه لكونه مرادفاً له الا ان دلالته تفصيلية ودلالة الكلى الجمالية فان الكلى كما ذكر هو مفهوم لا يمتنع فرض صدقه على كثيرين ای هو صالح بمجرد تصوره للحمل عليها وهذا هو المراد من المقول على كثيرين كما لا يخفى و لذا اعترض الامام الرازي على الشيخ بان زيادة لفظ «الكلى» غير محتاج اليه لانه كالمرادف للمقول على كثيرين. وكذا بعض المحققين فى شرح الرسالة حيث قال ان لفظ الكلى مستدرك و المقول على كثيرين جنس للخمسة ويخرج بالكثيرين الجزئى لانه مقول على واحد فيقال: هذا زيد. اقول والحق ان لفظ الكلى لا بدمنه في تعريف الكليات والا لم يطرد رسومها لصدقها على حدود الانواع والاجناس والفصول وغيرها اذ كما يصدق على الانسان انه المقول على الكثرة المتفقة الحقايق في جواب ما هو يصدق على حده اعنى الحيوان الناطق و كذا فى البواقى فلو لم يذكر لفظ الكلى في رسوم الكليات، لزم ان يكون حدود الانواع انواعاً وحدود الاجناس اجناساً وهكذا وليس كذلك بخلاف مالو ذكر فانه لا يصدق على شيء من حدودها انه كلى فان قيد الافراد معتبر فيه دون المقول على الكثرة و لذا ذكر بعض المحققين ان المقول على الكثرة اعم من الكلى. اللهم الا ان يدعى ان قيد الافراد معتبر في المقول على الكثرة ايضاً كما يظهر من بعضهم والله اعلم . لا يقال : لعل مذهب المصنف ان حدود الكليات داخلة تحت الكليات المساواتها لها في الصدق فلذا ترك لفظ الكلى مع كونه مذكوراً في كلمات الاكثرين. لا نا نقول : ان ذلك التزام المخالفة القوم من غير ضرورة داعية لذلك ولو سلم فيفوت المقابلة بين الكليات و حدودها فلابد اما من تقدير لفظ الكلى او تقييد المقول بالافراد فتأمل. ثم ان عبارة الاكثرين في هذا المقام المقول على كثيرين، عدل عنها المصنف الى ذلك لما يرد عليها ظاهراً من ان افراد الكلى يجب ان لا يكون اقل من سنة فان اقل الجمع ان يكون ثلاثة مقادير مفرده والكثرة لا تطلق على اقل من اثنين فانها مقابلة للوحدة و من انها يجب ان يكون من ذوى العقول قضاء لحق الجمع بالواو والنون كما صرح به النحويون و من هذا ظهر انه لو قال فيها قبل في تقسيم الكلى والجزئى: «المفهوم ان امتنع فرض صدقه على الكثرة» بدل قوله : «على كثيرين» لكان أولى وقد سبق هنالك وجه التقصى عن ذلك فتذكر.
And it appears from some of them that its omission is to avoid the need for it, for 'al-maqul 'ala al-kathra' suffices it because it is synonymous with it, except that its indication is detailed and the indication of 'al-kulli' is total. For al-kulli, as mentioned, is a concept that does not prevent assuming its truth of many—i.e., it is fit by mere conception to be predicated of them—and this is what is meant by 'al-maqul 'ala al-kathra' as is not hidden. Hence Imam al-Razi objected to al-Shaykh that adding the word 'al-kulli' is unnecessary because it is synonymous with 'al-maqul 'ala al-kathra'. And similarly some investigators in the commentary on the Epistle said that the word 'al-kulli' is redundant, and 'al-maqul 'ala al-kathra' is the genus for the five, and by 'kathra' the particular is excluded because it is predicated of one, e.g. 'this is Zayd'. I say: The truth is that the word 'al-kulli' is necessary in the definition of the universals; otherwise their definitions would not be exclusive because they are true of the limits of species, genera, differentiae, etc. For just as 'al-maqul 'ala al-kathra al-muttafiqa al-haqa'iq fi jawab ma huwa' is true of man, it is true of his limit, i.e., 'animal rational', and likewise for the rest. So if 'al-kulli' were not mentioned in the definitions of the universals, it would follow that the limits of species are species, and the limits of genera are genera, and so on, which is not the case—contrary to when it is mentioned, for then it is not true of any of their limits that it is kulli, because the condition of individuals is considered in it but not in 'al-maqul 'ala al-kathra'. And therefore some investigators said that 'al-maqul 'ala al-kathra' is more general than 'al-kulli'. Unless it is claimed that the condition of individuals is also considered in 'al-maqul 'ala al-kathra', as appears from some. And Allah knows best. It cannot be said: Perhaps the author's view is that the limits of the universals fall under the universals because they equal them in truth, so he omitted the word 'al-kulli' even though it is mentioned in the words of the majority. For we say: That would entail opposing the people without necessity. And if it is granted the comparison between the universals and their limits would be lost. So either the word 'al-kulli' must be assumed, or 'al-maqul' must be restricted to individuals. So reflect. Then the expression of the majority in this context is 'al-maqul 'ala al-kathra'; the author changed it to that because of what appears against it: that the individuals of a universal must not be less than six, for the least of a plural is three times its singular, and 'kathra' is not applied to less than two, for it is opposite to unity; and that it must be of those possessing intellect, doing justice to the plural with waw and nun, as the grammarians stated. From this it appears that if he had said previously in the division of universal and particular: 'the concept whose assumption of truth of many is prevented' instead of his saying 'upon many', it would be more appropriate, and the way to avoid that has preceded there. So remember.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) defending the inclusion of the word 'كلى' (universal) in the definitions of the five universals. The author starts by referencing some scholars who think the word is redundant because 'المقول على كثيرين' (predicated of many) is synonymous. However, the author points out that the two terms differ: 'كلى' indicates the concept's fitness for predication by mere conception, while 'المقول على كثيرين' indicates actual predication in answer to 'what is it?' which is more detailed. Imam al-Razi objected to al-Shaykh (likely Ibn Sina or al-Abhari) that 'كلى' is unnecessary, and some commentators on the Isaghuji said the same. The author counters that without 'كلى', the definitions would apply to the limits (حدود) of the universals. For example, 'animal rational' (the limit of human) would itself be a universal, leading to circularity. He argues that the condition of individuals being multiple is essential to 'كلى' but not to 'المقول على كثيرين', which can apply to a single thing in a certain sense. Then he addresses a potential objection: perhaps the author of the matn omitted 'كلى' intentionally because he considered the limits of universals to be included under the universals. The reply is that this would cause a loss of contrast between universals and their limits, and one must either assume the word 'كلى' or restrict 'المقول' to individuals. Finally, the author criticizes the phrase 'المقول على كثيرين' on linguistic grounds: 'كثرة' (many) implies at least three, whereas individuals of a universal may be fewer, and the plural form should be for rational beings. He suggests an alternative phrasing used earlier in the text. This lesson is a dense example of technical refinement in the logical tradition.

Key Terms
كلى
المقول على كثيرين
حدود الكليات

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The definitions would only apply to particulars.
D) No consequence.

Key Takeaway
The term 'kulli' is not redundant in the definitions of the five universals because it carries a condition of multiple individuals that prevents the definitions from applying to the limits of the universals themselves.

Resolving the Predication Paradox: Mental vs. External
Author addresses the objection that genus cannot be both part of essence and predicated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mental and external predication and use this distinction to resolve why genus can be a part of essence yet predicated.

Core Idea
Predication and part-hood are compatible across the mental/external divide.

The logician's definition of genus as 'al-maqul' faces a paradox: if genus is a part of the species' essence, how can it be predicated of the whole? This lesson examines the objection and the author's elegant solution based on the distinction between mental and external predication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
The objector argues: genus is a part of the essence; a part cannot be predicated of the whole because predication requires unity between subject and predicate. The example given: 'Sikanjabin is honey/vinegar' is invalid because honey/vinegar are parts, not the whole.
This is a standard objection in Islamic logic.
2
Understand the Necessity of Unity
Predication (al-haml) requires that the subject and predicate are one in existence in some respect. If genus is a part, it is not identical to the whole externally, so external predication seems impossible.
3
Analyze the Author's Distinction
The author distinguishes mental (dhihn) from external (kharij) predication. In the mind, genus is a part of the species' definition. In external predication, the necessity of unity applies only externally. Thus, mental part-hood does not prevent external predication.
Example: 'Human is an animal' – animal is part of human's mental definition but externally predicated of human.
4
Apply the Distinction
Test with an Islamic science example: in Usul al-Fiqh, the genus 'illah (cause) is part of the definition of qiyas mentally, but externally we say 'this analogy has an illah' – predication holds externally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بق هنا شيء وهو: ان الجنس جزء الماهية كما ذكر وجزء الشيء لا يكون محمولاً عليه لوجوب الاتحاد بين المحكوم عليه و به كما صرح به غير واحد فلا يجوز تعريفه بالمقول كما هو ظاهر. الا ترى انه لا يجوزان يقال : السكنجبين عسل اوخل ؟ والجواب ان ان وجوب الاتحاد بين المحكوم عليه و به انما هو بحسب الخارج دون الذهن فلا ينافيه الجزئية بحسب الذهن كما في الجنس بخلاف المثال المذكور فان جزئيته بحسب الخارج أيضاً. فالمراد بقوله: (( المقول» هو المحمول بحسب الخارج. لا يقال: ان من الاجناس مالا يوجد له فرد فى الخارج حتى يكون محمولاً عليه بحسب الخارج فيخرج عن التعريف على ما ذكر. لانا نقول : لانسلم ذلك فان جميع الاجناس يجب ان يكون محمولاً بحسب الخارج لكن لا تحقيقاً بل فرضاً بمعنى ان العقل يفرض له فرداً خارجياً ثم يجعله محمولاً عليه بحسب الخارج وذلك الفرض قد يكون مطابقاً للواقع وقد لا يكون وبمامر سابقاً من معنى الفرض و بيان المراد منه لا يتوجه اعتراض بعض المحققين من شراح المتن بان اعتبار المقول على الكثرة بالفرض العقلى يستلزم جواز اجتماع الكليات الخمس فى مفهوم واحد بحسب الفروض المختلفة فلا يمكن تخصيص شيء من المفهومات بشيء من اقسام الكليات بل ذلك راجع الى الفرض العقلى على هذا التقدير. انتهى
There remains here something: that genus is part of the essence as mentioned, and a part of a thing is not predicated of it because of the necessity of unity between the subject and predicate, as stated by more than one scholar, so it is not permissible to define it as 'al-maqul' as is apparent. Do you not see that it is not permissible to say: 'Sikanjabin is honey or vinegar'? The answer is that the necessity of unity between subject and predicate is only with respect to the external world (kharij), not the mind (dhihn), so it does not contradict essentiality with respect to the mind, as in the case of genus—unlike the aforementioned example, for its part-hood is also with respect to the external. So what is meant by 'al-maqul' is the predicate with respect to the external. It cannot be said: Among genera there are those that have no individual in the external world such that they could be predicated of it with respect to the external, so they would be excluded from the definition according to what was mentioned. For we say: We do not accept that. For all genera must be predicated with respect to the external, but not actually, rather hypothetically (farḍan), in the sense that the intellect hypothetically assumes an external individual for it, then makes it predicated of it with respect to the external. That assumption may match reality or may not. And what has previously passed about the meaning of 'farḍ' and the clarification of what is intended from it, the objection of some investigators among the commentators of the matn that considering 'al-maqul 'ala al-kathra' with hypothetical assumption necessitates the possibility of the five universals meeting in one concept according to different assumptions, so nothing of the concepts can be specified to any of the divisions of the universals—rather that reverts to the hypothetical assumption on this estimation—does not apply. Ended.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical response to an objection. The objector argues that genus cannot be defined as 'what is predicated' (المقول) because genus is a part of the essence, and a part cannot be predicated of the whole—predication requires unity of subject and predicate. The example given: you cannot say 'sikanjabin (a mixture) is honey or vinegar' because honey and vinegar are parts, not the whole. The author's response distinguishes between mental and external predication. The necessity of unity between subject and predicate applies only to external predication, not to mental. In the mind, genus is a part of the essence, but in external predication, it is predicated of the species. For example, 'animal' is part of the definition of 'human' in the mind, but when we say 'Human is an animal', we are predicating animal of human externally. The author then addresses a further objection: what about genera that have no actual external individuals? For instance, the genus 'body' might have no individual if no bodies exist? The author responds that all genera can be predicated hypothetically (فرضاً) even if no actual individual exists. The intellect can assume an individual and then predicate the genus of it. This hypothetical predication is enough for logical definitions. Finally, the author rejects an objection by some commentators that this hypothetical approach would make the five universals unstable, because the assumption of individuals is tied to the hypothetical act, not to the concept itself. Thus, the definition holds.

Key Terms
جزء
محمول
خارج
فرض

A) Mental
B) External
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Genus is a part of the species in the mind but predicated externally, resolving the apparent contradiction.

Hypothetical Predication and the Stability of the Five Universals
Author addresses the objection that genus cannot be both part of essence and predicated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how hypothetical predication (farḍ) allows empty genera to be predicated and why this does not destabilize the five universals.

Core Idea
Hypothetical predication saves definitions for empty genera without altering conceptual content.

A further objection asks: what if a genus has no actual individuals in the external world? How can it be predicated? The author introduces hypothetical predication (farḍ) and then addresses a concern raised by commentators that this would make the five universals relative to assumptions.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the Problem of Empty Genera
Some genera (e.g., 'jism' if no bodies existed) may have no actual external individuals. If predication requires an actual external subject, these genera would be excluded from the definition of 'al-maqul'.
2
Understand the Author's Solution: Hypothetical Predication
The author argues that predication need not be actual (tahqiqan) but can be hypothetical (farḍan). The intellect hypothetically assumes an external individual for the genus and predicates the genus of that assumed individual.
This is not a 'fiction' but a logical tool to ensure definability.
3
Analyze the Commentators' Objection
Some commentators argued that if predication depends on hypothetical assumption, then the five universals could be applied arbitrarily to any concept under different assumptions, making classification unstable.
4
Examine the Author's Rejection
The author rejects this objection because the hypothetical assumption does not change the conceptual content of the universal. The assumption only provides a subject for predication; the universal's nature remains fixed. Thus, the five universals remain stable.
The key: hypothetical assumption is a testing tool, not a redefinition.

Distinguishing Kulli and Maqul 'ala al-Kathra
Explanation of why 'kulli' is omitted and the relation between two universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'kulli' and 'maqul 'ala al-kathra' based on the condition of individuals and explain why the latter is more general.

Core Idea
'Kulli' requires individuals; 'maqul 'ala al-kathra' does not — one is narrower.

In defining the five universals, precision matters. The term 'kulli' includes a condition that it applies to individuals, while the phrase 'maqul 'ala al-kathra' is broader. Understanding this distinction sharpens your grasp of the universal categories and their use in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key terms
Locate the terms 'kulli' (universal) and 'maqul 'ala al-kathra' (said of many) in the Arabic passage.
They appear at the beginning and middle of the passage.
2
Find the condition of individuals
Look for the phrase 'qayd al-afrad' (condition of individuals). The commentator states it is present in 'kulli' but not in 'maqul 'ala al-kathra'.
3
Understand the consequence
Because 'kulli' adds this condition, 'maqul 'ala al-kathra' is more general (a'am). Some investigators explicitly state this.
4
Consider the counterclaim
The author notes that some might argue the condition of individuals is also present in 'maqul 'ala al-kathra'. Evaluate which view is more convincing based on the text.
The phrase 'Allahu a'lam' indicates humility, but the author leans toward the distinction.

ذکر فانه لا يصدق على شيء من حدودها انه كلى فان قيد الافراد معتبر فيه دون المقول على الكثرة و لذا ذكر بعض المحققين ان المقول على الكثرة اعم من الكلى. اللهم الا ان يدعى ان قيد الافراد معتبر في المقول على الكثرة ايضاً كما يظهر من بعضهم والله اعلم .
He mentioned that it (the definition) does not apply to any of its limits that it is universal, for the condition of individuals is considered in it but not in what is said of many. Hence some investigators mentioned that 'what is said of many' is more general than the universal. Unless it is claimed that the condition of individuals is also considered in 'what is said of many', as appears from some of them. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a critical note on the definition of genus (or a universal) that omitted the term 'kulli' (universal). The commentator explains that the omission is possible because 'kulli' includes a condition of applicability to individuals (qayd al-afrad), whereas the phrase 'maqul 'ala al-kathra' (said of many) does not necessarily include that condition. Therefore, some scholars conclude that 'maqul 'ala al-kathra' is broader than 'kulli'. However, the author notes a counterclaim: some might argue that the condition of individuals is also present in 'maqul 'ala al-kathra'. The passage thus highlights a nuance in the definitions of the five universals.

Why the Definitions of Universals Are Not Themselves Universals
Objection that the author may have included limits of universals under universals, and the answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the definitions (limits) of the five universals are not themselves universals, and what textual adjustments are needed to preserve the contrast.

Core Idea
Definitions of universals are not universals; mixing them breaks the conceptual contrast.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a subtle but important objection: perhaps the author omitted the word 'kulli' because he considered the definitions of universals to be themselves universals. The commentator argues that this would be a mistake, as it would destroy the intended contrast between each universal and its definition. Understanding this distinction is crucial for precise logical terminology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
The opponent proposes that the author may have omitted 'kulli' because he believed the definitions (limits) of the universals are themselves universals, since they are equally true of the same things. Find this in the Arabic after 'la yuqalu'.
The objection claims that if the definitions are co-extensive with the universals, they could be considered universals themselves.
2
Analyze the Commentator's Response
The commentator first says this would unnecessarily contradict the majority opinion, and more importantly, it would break the 'muqabala' (contrast) between the universals and their definitions. What does 'muqabala' mean here? It means the parallel listing of the five universals alongside their definitions.
If the definitions were themselves universals, they would be included in the same category, ruining the structure of the discussion.
3
Understand the Proposed Solutions
The commentator suggests two fixes: either consider the word 'kulli' as implied in the author's definition, or qualify the phrase 'what is said' by adding 'individuals' to make clear that only individuals are meant, not the definitions themselves. Reflect on why these preserve the contrast.
Both solutions ensure that the definitions are not mistaken for the universals they define.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : لعل مذهب المصنف ان حدود الكليات داخلة تحت الكليات المساواتها لها في الصدق فلذا ترك لفظ الكلى مع كونه مذكوراً في كلمات الاكثرين. قلنا : ان ذلك التزام المخالفة القوم من غير ضرورة داعية لذلك ولو سلم فيفوت المقابلة بين الكليات و حدودها فلابد اما من تقدير لفظ الكلى او تقييد المقول بالافراد فتأمل.
It cannot be said: Perhaps the author's view is that the limits of universals are included under the universals because of their equality in truthfulness, so he omitted the term 'universal' even though it is mentioned in the words of the majority. We say: That would entail contradicting the people without necessity, and if conceded, the contrast between the universals and their limits would be lost. Therefore, one must either consider the term 'universal' as implied or qualify 'what is said' with 'individuals'. So reflect.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange (naqd wa jawab). The opponent suggests that the author omitted 'kulli' because he believed the definitions (limits) of universals are themselves universals, since they have the same extension. The commentator responds that this would unnecessarily contradict the majority view, and more importantly, it would break the intended contrast between the universals (e.g., genus, species) and their definitions. To preserve the contrast, one must either assume 'kulli' is understood or add the condition 'individuals' to 'maqul'. This shows the care taken in the terminology of the five universals.

Key Takeaway
Definitions of universals must be kept distinct from the universals themselves to preserve the logical structure of the five universals.

Refining the Phrase for Universals: From 'Kathirin' to 'Kathra'
Critique of the phrase 'said of many' and recommendation for 'said of the many'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the linguistic issues with the phrase 'maqul 'ala kathirin' and justify the alternative 'maqul 'ala al-kathra'.

Core Idea
Universal terms must fit all individuals; 'kathirin' fails due to number and rationality constraints.

When defining universals, even word choice matters. Earlier logicians used 'maqul 'ala kathirin' but this phrase carries hidden grammatical assumptions. The author replaced it with 'maqul 'ala al-kathra' to avoid these issues. Understanding this refinement teaches you to critically evaluate technical terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criticized phrase
Locate 'المقول على كثيرين' (al-maqul 'ala kathirin) in the Arabic text. This is the phrase used by the majority that the author changes.
2
Understand the first objection
The word 'كثيرين' is a sound masculine plural. Arabic grammarians state that the minimum for a plural is three. But a universal can apply to just two individuals (e.g., the universal 'human' applies to Zayd and Amr).
The passage mentions 'اقل الجمع ان يكون ثلاثة' (the least of a plural is three).
3
Understand the second objection
The sound masculine plural with واو ونون is typically used for rational beings. But universals apply to non-rationals too (e.g., 'horse'). So the phrase incorrectly restricts universals.
4
Evaluate the alternative 'al-kathra'
The word 'الكثرة' (al-kathra) means 'many' in an indefinite sense without specifying number or rationality. It avoids both objections: it does not imply a minimum of three nor rationality.
5
Apply the lesson to the definition of universal
The commentator suggests an even better phrasing: 'المفهوم ان امتنع فرض صدقه على الكثرة' (the concept whose assumption of truth upon many is prevented). This directly avoids the issues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ان عبارة الاكثرين في هذا المقام المقول على كثيرين، عدل عنها المصنف الى ذلك لما يرد عليها ظاهراً من ان افراد الكلى يجب ان لا يكون اقل من سنة فان اقل الجمع ان يكون ثلاثة مقادير مفرده والكثرة لا تطلق على اقل من اثنين فانها مقابلة للوحدة و من انها يجب ان يكون من ذوى العقول قضاء لحق الجمع بالواو والنون كما صرح به النحويون و من هذا ظهر انه لو قال فيها قبل في تقسيم الكلى والجزئى: «المفهوم ان امتنع فرض صدقه على الكثرة» بدل قوله : «على كثيرين» لكان أولى وقد سبق هنالك وجه التقصى عن ذلك فتذكر.
Then the expression of the majority in this place is 'said of many' (maqul 'ala kathirin), but the author changed it to 'that' (al-kathra) due to an apparent objection that the individuals of the universal must not be less than three, for the least of a plural is three times its singular, and 'kathra' (many) is not applied to less than two because it is opposite to unity; and also that it must be of rational beings to fulfill the right of the plural with waw and nun as the grammarians have stated. From this it appears that if he had said earlier in the division of universal and particular: 'the concept which prevents its assumption of truth upon many' instead of his saying 'upon many', it would have been better. And the way to avoid that has already been mentioned there, so remember.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage criticizes the phrase 'maqul 'ala kathirin' (said of many) used by the majority. The author replaced it with 'maqul 'ala al-kathra' (said of many in a general sense) for two reasons. First, 'kathirin' implies at least two, but a plural (jam') requires at least three. Second, the plural form with waw and nun (sound masculine plural) is typically used for rational beings. Therefore, the phrase is not suitable for all universals. The commentator suggests a better formulation: 'the concept which prevents the assumption of its truth upon many' (al-mafhum in imtana'a fardh sidqihi 'ala al-kathra). This avoids the issues.

Key Terms
المقول على كثيرين
الكثرة

B) Because 'al-kathra' is a more precise term.
C) Because the majority used 'al-kathra'.
D) Because 'kathirin' is a grammatical error.

Key Takeaway
Technical terms in logic must be scrutinized; Arabic grammar can reveal hidden assumptions that require refinement.

Genus as Part in Mind, Predicated in Reality
Objection that genus cannot be predicated because it is a part, and the answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why genus can be predicated of its species despite being a part of the essence, using the distinction between mental and external union.

Core Idea
Genus is a part only mentally; predication requires external union.

This passage from al-Yazdi's Hashiya addresses a critical objection to the definition of genus as 'said of many'. If genus is a part of the species' essence, how can it be predicated? The answer hinges on a key distinction between mental and external union. Understanding this resolves a fundamental tension in the theory of predicables.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection states that if genus is a part of the species' essence, it cannot be predicated of the species because predication requires a union (ittihad) between subject and predicate. A part cannot be in union with the whole.
The example given is oxymel (sakanjabin): it is a mixture of honey and vinegar, so you cannot say 'oxymel is honey' because honey is only a part.
2
Extract the distinction
The commentator responds by distinguishing external (bi-hasab al-kharij) and mental (bi-hasab al-dhihn) union. The necessity of union for predication applies only to external existence, not mental existence.
Genus is a part of the species only in mental analysis (when defining), not externally in reality.
3
Apply to the genus-species relation
Thus, predicating 'animal' of 'human' is valid because externally, human is an animal (the whole), while mentally, 'animal' is a part of the definition of 'human'. The example of oxymel is different because honey and vinegar are also externally parts of the mixture.
The example fails to parallel genus because in genus, the part-whole relation is only mental.
4
Address the counter-objection about non-existent individuals
An objector says some genera have no individuals in external existence, so they cannot be predicated externally. The commentator replies that predication is not actual but hypothetical (bi-l-fard): the intellect supposes an individual and then predicates the genus upon it.
This mental supposition (al-fardh al-'aqli) allows the definition to cover all genera, even those without actual individuals.
5
Refute the charge that this conflates the five universals
Some critics argued that if predication is by mental supposition, then any concept could be considered any universal based on different suppositions. The commentator dismisses this because the mental supposition is constrained by the nature of the concept and the rules of the universals.
The earlier clarification of 'supposition' prevents this misinterpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بق هنا شيء وهو: ان الجنس جزء الماهية كما ذكر وجزء الشيء لا يكون محمولاً عليه لوجوب الاتحاد بين المحكوم عليه و به كما صرح به غیر واحد فلا يجوز تعريفه بالمقول كما هو ظاهر. الا ترى انه لا يجوزان يقال : السكنجبين عسل اوخل ؟ والجواب ان ان وجوب الاتحاد بين المحكوم عليه و به انما هو بحسب الخارج دون الذهن فلا ينافيه الجزئية بحسب الذهن كما في الجنس بخلاف المثال المذكور فان جزئيته بحسب الخارج أيضاً. فالمراد بقوله: (( المقول» هو المحمول بحسب الخارج. لا يقال: ان من الاجناس مالا يوجد له فرد فى الخارج حتى يكون محمولاً عليه بحسب الخارج فيخرج عن التعريف على ما ذكر. لانا نقول : لانسلم ذلك فان جميع الاجناس يجب ان يكون محمولاً بحسب الخارج لكن لا تحقيقاً بل فرضاً بمعنى ان العقل يفرض له فرداً خارجياً ثم يجعله محمولاً عليه بحسب الخارج وذلك الفرض قد يكون مطابقاً للواقع وقد لا يكون وبمامر سابقاً من معنى الفرض و بيان المراد منه لا يتوجه اعتراض بعض المحققين من شراح المتن بان اعتبار المقول على الكثرة بالفرض العقلى يستلزم جواز اجتماع الكليات الخمس فى مفهوم واحد بحسب الفروض المختلفة فلا يمكن تخصيص شيء من المفهومات بشيء من اقسام الكليات بل ذلك راجع الى الفرض العقلى على هذا التقدير. انتهى
Here remains something: that genus is a part of the essence as mentioned, and a part of a thing cannot be predicated of it because of the necessity of union between subject and predicate, as many have stated. So it is not permissible to define it as 'said of', as is apparent. Do you not see that it is not permissible to say: 'al-sakanjabin (oxymel) is honey or vinegar?' The answer is that the necessity of union between subject and predicate is according to external existence, not mental existence. So it is not incompatible with being a part in the mental, as in the case of genus, unlike the mentioned example, for its part is external as well. So what is meant by 'al-maqul' (the said) is the predicate according to external existence. It cannot be said: there are some genera that have no individual in external existence so that it can be predicated of it externally, thus they would be excluded from the definition according to what has been mentioned. We say: we do not accept that, for all genera must be predicated externally, but not actually, rather hypothetically, meaning that the intellect supposes an external individual for it and then makes it a predicate according to external existence. That supposition may correspond to reality or not. With what has passed earlier about the meaning of supposition and clarification of what is meant from it, the objection of some investigators from the commentators of the text, that considering 'said of many' as mental supposition would necessitate the possibility of the five universals gathering in one concept according to different suppositions, so it would not be possible to specify any concept as a particular kind of universal, but that would be due to the mental supposition on this estimation, does not apply. End.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage addresses a problem: if genus is a part of the essence, how can it be predicated of the species? Predication requires union (ittihad) between subject and predicate. An example: oxymel (sikanjabin) is a mixture of honey and vinegar; you cannot say 'oxymel is honey' or 'oxymel is vinegar' because each is a part, not the whole. The answer distinguishes external and mental union. In the case of genus, the union is external (the species exists as a whole) but in the mind, the genus is a part of the definition. This does not violate the requirement of external union. Then an objection: some genera have no external individuals (e.g., 'phoenix'? but Islamic logic example: 'human' genus? Actually genera like 'body' have individuals). The commentator answers that predication is not actual but hypothetical (bi-l-fard): the intellect supposes an individual and predicates the genus. This does not lead to the criticism that the five universals could mix, because the supposition is consistent.

Key Takeaway
Genus can be predicated of its species because the required union is external, while the part-whole relation is mental; for genera without individuals, mental supposition allows predication.

Why 'Kulli' is Essential in the Definition of Species
Refinement of the definition of species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the term 'kulli' (universal) is necessary in the definition of species and apply it to exclude the limits (definitions) of species from being considered species themselves.

Core Idea
Without 'kulli', the definition of species would mistakenly include the definitions of species.

The definition of species as 'said of many agreeing in quiddity in answer to what is it' seems clear, but the commentator points out a subtle flaw: without the qualifier 'kulli', the definition would apply to the definitions of species, which are not themselves species. This lesson unpacks that logical refinement and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of species from the matn
Locate the Arabic definition: 'al-naw' wa huwa al-maqul 'ala al-kathra al-muttafiqa al-haqiqa fi jawab ma huwa'. Note that it includes 'said of many' but not 'kulli'.
The initial definition is incomplete for the commentator.
2
Understand the problem: what gets included without 'kulli'
If we replace the term 'species' with its definition in a proposition like 'the definition of man is a species', the definition would apply to the definition itself (e.g., 'rational animal' would be a species), which is not intended. The commentator says: 'the definitions of genera would be genus, and definitions of species would be species'.
The issue is self-referential application of the definition.
3
See how 'kulli' resolves the problem
Adding 'kulli' restricts the definition to universal concepts that are not themselves definitions. A definition like 'rational animal' is a universal, but it is not said of many individuals in answer to 'what is it?' in the same way; it is a second-order universal.
'Kulli' excludes the limits (hudud) of species.
4
Compare with al-Razi's objection
Al-Razi considered 'maqul 'ala al-kathra' synonymous with 'kulli', so he thought the addition was redundant. The commentator argues that 'kulli' is more general because there are universals not said of many (e.g., the concept of 'one' might be a universal but not predicated of many).
This highlights the need for precision in logical definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

لان تعريف الجنس يصدق على تعريف الاجناس و كذلك تعريف النوع يصدق على حدود الانواع وهكذا تعريف الخاصة والعرض العام اذ كما ان الحيوان مقول على الانسان و الفرس، كذلك حد الحيوان و كما ان الانسان مقول على زيد و بشر وغيرهما، كذلك حد الانسان تأمل فلزم ان يكون حدود الاجناس جنساً و حدود الانواع نوعاً و ليس كذلك، فلابد من ذكر الكلى لاخراج ذلك و المقول على الكثرة لا يخرج ذلك ، لان قيد الافراد معتبر في الكلى المقول على الكثرة كالمرادف للكلى فان الكلى اعم منه لوجود كلى غير مقول على الكثرة فتأمل. و اعلم ان لفظ النوع كان معناه فى الوضع الأول عند اليونانيين حقيقة الشيء وماهيته والمنطقيون وجد وا ماهيات للاشياء ينضم الى الاجناس فيحصل الانواع فلذا قدمه المصنف تبعاً لهم واما المتأخرون فهم يقدمون النوع الشرفه (عبدالرحيم (ره) وقال الشيخ محمد على ره فى هذا المقام ما هذا الفظه ) : قد سبق فى تعريف الجنس ما يجديك فى فى الجواب و اذا ضممنا اليه الحجر يقع الجسم المطلق في الجواب وهكذا فيصدق على كل واحد منها انه المقول على الكثرة المتفقة الحقيقة في جواب ما هو مع انها ليست بانواع بل اجناس. والجواب ان المراد ان النوع هو المقول على الكثرة المتفقة الحقيقة فقط اى من غير ان يضم اليها شيء من الأمور المختلفة لها في الحقيقة فيخرج ما ذكر عن تعريف النوع فان الحيوان مثلاً انما يقال على الامور المتفقة الحقيقة مع ضميمة الفرس حتى لو اكتفى بها ولا يضم اليها شيء مما يخالفها في الماهية لا يقع
On the five universals. The second: the species, and it is that which is said of many agreeing in quiddity in answer to 'what is it', and it may be said of the essence. His saying: 'and it may be said of the essence' means 'said in answer to what is it', so it is nothing but the species. But there is a consideration: for the definition of genus applies to the definitions of genera, and likewise the definition of species applies to the limits of species, and similarly for the definition of property and common accident, because just as 'animal' is said of man and horse, so too the definition of animal; and just as 'man' is said of Zayd and Bishr and others, so too the definition of man. So reflect, for then the limits of genera would be genus, and limits of species would be species, but it is not so. Therefore, it is necessary to mention 'universal' to exclude that, and 'said of many' does not exclude that, because the condition of individuals is considered in 'universal' but not in 'said of many', so 'said of many' is more general than 'universal' in this respect. And Imam al-Razi went to that 'said of many' is synonymous with 'universal', so he objected to the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) that the addition of 'universal' is unnecessary. But we do not accept that 'said of many' is synonymous with 'universal', for the universal is more general than it because there exists a universal that is not said of many. So reflect. And know that the word 'species' in its first usage among the Greeks meant the reality of a thing and its quiddity, and the logicians found quiddities for things under the genus, so they transferred it to them, and used it by homonymy for two different meanings: one called 'true species' and the other 'relative species'. Then know that the practice of the ancients is to present genus before species because of its priority in mind and in existence, for differentiae join genera to produce species, so the author followed them in that. As for the later scholars, they present species because of its honor. (Abd al-Rahim (ra)) And Shaykh Muhammad Ali (ra) said in this place: 'What has preceded in the definition of genus suffices for you in this context, so we will not repeat it for fear of lengthening speech, but here is something that should be pointed out: what is said when we ask: "Zayd and Amr and Bakr and the horse, what are they?" The answer is "animal". And if we add to that the tree, the answer is "growing body", and if we add stone, the answer is "absolute body", and so on. So each of them would be said to be 'said of many agreeing in quiddity in answer to what is it' despite being genera, not species. The answer is that what is intended is that the species is said of many agreeing in quiddity only, i.e., without including any differing things, so the mentioned examples are excluded from the definition of species. For 'animal', for example, is said of things agreeing in quiddity with the inclusion of the horse, so if one were to rely only on that and not include anything differing in quiddity, it would not occur...'
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the definition of species (al-naw'). It begins with the standard definition: 'said of many agreeing in quiddity in answer to what is it'. The commentator notes that the term 'universal' (kulli) is needed to exclude the definitions (limits) of species from being considered species themselves. Without 'kulli', the definition of species would apply to the definitions of species, which is not intended. He then discusses the view of Imam al-Razi that 'maqul 'ala al-kathra' is synonymous with 'kulli', but the commentator disagrees, arguing that 'kulli' is more general because there are universals not said of many (e.g., perhaps the concept of 'one'?). Then there is a note on the two meanings of 'naw'': true species (naw' haqiqi) and relative species (naw' idafi). Finally, there is a remark about the order of presentation (genus before species) and a quotation from Shaykh Muhammad 'Ali addressing an objection: if we ask about Zayd, Amr, Bakr, and the horse, the answer is 'animal', which is a genus, not a species. The answer clarifies that the definition of species requires that the things are only agreeing in quiddity without any other differing items being included; in the example, 'animal' is said of those individuals only when horse is included, which is a different quiddity, so it is not a species. The text ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear.

Key Terms
النوع
الكلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the term 'kulli' (universal) necessary in the definition of species according to the commentator?
A) Because 'maqul 'ala al-kathra' is ambiguous.
B) To exclude the definitions (limits) of species from being considered species themselves.
C) Because without it, the definition would apply to genera.
D) Because al-Razi argued it is unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
The definition of species must include 'kulli' to prevent the definitions of species from being classified as species themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the definition of 'hadd' (legal punishment) in fiqh: 'a predetermined punishment for a transgression against God's rights'. Check whether the definition would mistakenly include discretionary punishments (ta'zir) if the qualifier 'predetermined' were omitted.

The Five Universals
beginner
6 min
True Species vs. Relative Species: Two Meanings of al-Naw'
Two meanings of the term 'species'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between true species (al-naw' al-haqiqi) and relative species (al-naw' al-idafi) and explain the historical shift in the meaning of the term 'naw'.

Core Idea
The word 'species' has two meanings: the ultimate species and a species that can also be a genus.

The commentator notes that the term 'naw'' was originally used by Greek philosophers to mean the reality of a thing, but logicians transferred it to mean a species under a genus. This led to two distinct concepts: true species (the ultimate species) and relative species (a species that can be a genus relative to lower species). This lesson clarifies these two usages.

Guided Path
1
Understand the original Greek usage
The commentator states that among the Greeks, 'naw'' originally meant the reality (haqiqa) and quiddity (mahiya) of a thing. It was not a logical term.
The shift is from ontological to logical.
2
See the logicians' transfer
Logicians found that things under a genus have quiddities, so they transferred the word 'naw'' to those quiddities. They used it homonymously for two meanings.
Homonymy (ishtirak lafzi) means the same word for different concepts.
3
Define true species (al-naw' al-haqiqi)
True species is the lowest species that cannot be further subdivided into sub-species. It is the ultimate species in a classification tree.
Example: 'man' is a true species under 'animal'.
4
Define relative species (al-naw' al-idafi)
Relative species is a species that can also be considered a genus relative to lower species. For example, 'animal' is a genus relative to 'man' but a species relative to 'body'.
The same concept can be both genus and species depending on the level of classification.
5
Apply to an Islamic science example
In fiqh, 'wajib' is a species under 'hukm taklifi', but it can also be a genus for 'wajib 'ayni' and 'wajib kifa'i'.
This shows the relative nature of the term.

Source Text

Distinguishing Genus from Species in Practice
Objection and answer about animal being a genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to resolve the objection that 'animal' said of many individuals would be a species according to the definition, and correctly distinguish between genus and species in similar cases.

Core Idea
The definition of species requires agreement in quiddity alone, without inclusion of differing quiddities.

An apparent problem arises: if we ask about Zayd, Amr, Bakr, and a horse, the answer is 'animal', which is a genus. But 'animal' is said of many agreeing in quiddity? The objection is resolved by noting that the definition of species requires the predicate to apply to things that agree in quiddity exclusively, without including any other differing quiddities. This lesson walks through that resolution.

Guided Path
1
State the objection clearly
The objection says: 'animal' is said of Zayd, Amr, Bakr (humans) and a horse; all agree in being animals. According to the definition of species (without further qualification), 'animal' would be a species, but it is actually a genus.
This seems to contradict the definition.
2
Identify the subtlety in the definition
The definition of species is 'said of many agreeing in quiddity in answer to what is it' – but the commentator clarifies that it must be 'only' (faqat) agreeing in quiddity, meaning no other differing quiddities are included in the predicate's extension.
The word 'only' is implied from the context.
3
Apply the clarification to the example
'Animal' is said of humans and horses, but humans and horses differ in quiddity (one is rational, the other irrational). Therefore, 'animal' is not said of things that agree in quiddity alone; it includes different types. Thus, it is a genus, not a species.
If we considered only humans, 'animal' would be too broad; if we consider the species 'man', it is said of humans only, agreeing in quiddity.
4
Generalize the rule
A predicate is a species if and only if it applies to individuals that all share the same quiddity, without any subgroup differing in quiddity. Otherwise, it is a higher universal (genus or differentia).
This is a key distinction in the theory of the five universals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary

Then there is a note on the two meanings of 'naw'': true species (naw' haqiqi) and relative species (naw' idafi). Finally, there is a remark about the order of presentation (genus before species) and a quotation from Shaykh Muhammad 'Ali addressing an objection: if we ask about Zayd, Amr, Bakr, and the horse, the answer is 'animal', which is a genus, not a species. The answer clarifies that the definition of species requires that the things are only agreeing in quiddity without any other differing items being included; in the example, 'animal' is said of those individuals only when horse is included, which is a different quiddity, so it is not a species. The text ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear.

The definition of species requires that the predicate apply to things that agree in quiddity exclusively; if it includes different quiddities, it is a genus.

Definition of Species and the Necessity of 'Kulli'
Author defines species and defends inclusion of 'kulli' in its definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the word 'kulli' is necessary in the definition of species and distinguish species from genera using the condition of 'only' (without adding different quiddities).

Core Idea
Without 'kulli', the definition of species would also fit definitions of genera.

In this passage, the author defines species (al-naw') but immediately tackles a crucial point: whether the word 'kulli' (universal) is needed in the definition. Some argue it is redundant, but the commentator demonstrates that without it, definitions of genera would also qualify as species. An objection forces a further refinement: species must be said of many things 'only', without adding any different quiddity. This lesson walks through the logical steps of that refinement.

Guided Path
1
Locate the base definition of species
Find the phrase 'المقول على الكثرة المتفقة الحقيقة في جواب ما هو' and note that it omits the word 'كلى'. This is the initial definition under debate.
The definition appears at the very beginning of the passage.
2
Identify the problem with omission
The commentator argues that without 'كلى', the definition would also apply to definitions of genera, e.g., 'حد الحيوان' is also said of many agreeing things in answer to 'what is it?'. So species would be confused with genus definitions.
This is the key objection to omitting 'كلى'.
3
Distinguish 'مقول على الكثرة' from 'كلى'
'مقول على الكثرة' is more general because it lacks the condition of individuals. 'كلى' includes that condition, thus excluding definitions of genera (which are said of many but do not apply to individuals as such).
Imam al-Razi considered them synonyms, but the commentator rejects that.
4
Analyze the objection about mixed groups
The objection says: when asked about a group including humans and a horse, the answer is 'animal', which is a genus, yet it satisfies 'said of many things agreeing in reality'. This seems to show the definition includes genera.
This objection targets the definition even with 'كلى'? Actually, it targets the definition without 'only'.
5
Examine the response: the condition 'only'
The response adds the word 'فقط' (only), meaning species is said of many things agreeing in reality without any addition of different quiddities. For 'animal' to be a species, it would have to be said of humans and horses only, with no other different thing added. Since 'animal' includes trees when added, it is a genus, not a species.
This refinement excludes genera by requiring that the predicate does not extend to things of different natures.

The second: Species, and it is that which is said of many things agreeing in reality in answer to 'what is it?', and it may be said of the quiddity. His statement: 'and it may be said of the quiddity' means that which is said in answer to 'what is it?', so it is only that which is said of many things. The omission of the word 'universal' in the definition of the universals due to sufficiency of 'said of many things' needs consideration because the definition of genus applies to the definitions of genera, and likewise the definition of species applies to the limits of species, and thus the definition of property and common accident, for just as 'animal' is said of man and horse, so is the definition of animal; and just as 'man' is said of Zayd and Bashar and others, so is the definition of man. Reflect: then it would follow that the limits of genera are genera and the limits of species are species, but it is not so. Therefore it is necessary to mention 'universal' to exclude that, and 'said of many things' does not exclude that, because the condition of individuals is considered in the universal but not in 'said of many things'; so 'said of many things' is more general than 'universal' in this consideration. Imam al-Razi held that 'said of many things' is synonymous with 'universal', and he objected to the teacher that the addition of 'universal' is unnecessary, and he understood what I mentioned, but we do not concede that 'said of many things' is synonymous with 'universal', for 'universal' is more general than it because there exists a universal not said of many things, so reflect. It has preceded in the definition of genus what suffices you in this topic, so we do not repeat it for fear of lengthening the speech, but here is something that should be alerted to, and it is that it is said: when we say 'Zayd, Amr, Bakr, and horse, what are they?' the answer occurs: 'animal'; and if we add to that 'tree', 'growing body' occurs in the answer; and if we add to it 'stone', 'absolute body' occurs in the answer; and so on, so each one of them is truly said of many things agreeing in reality in answer to 'what is it?' despite them not being species but genera. The answer is that what is meant is that species is that which is said of many things agreeing in reality only, i.e., without being joined to something among the different matters in reality, so what was mentioned is excluded from the definition of species, for 'animal', for example, is only said of things agreeing in reality with the addition of horse, so if it is sufficient with it and nothing opposed in quiddity is joined, the answer will not be 'animal' nor any other genus at all.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition of species (النوع) and a defense of including the word 'universal' (كلى) in its definition. The author first states that species is 'said of many things agreeing in reality in answer to what is it?' The commentator then notes that the phrase 'it may be said of the quiddity' means that the predicate is given in answer to 'what is it?'. The main issue is whether the word 'universal' is needed in the definition. Without it, the definition would also apply to definitions of genera (e.g., the definition of 'animal' is said of many individuals but is not a species). The commentator argues that 'said of many things' alone does not exclude such cases, while 'universal' does because it requires considering individuals. Imam al-Razi considered them synonymous, but the commentator rejects that. Then an objection is raised: when we ask about a group including humans and animals, the answer 'animal' applies, yet that group is not a species but a genus. The response is that species must be said of many things without any addition of different matters. For example, 'animal' is said of humans and horses only if no other different thing is added; otherwise, it is not a species. Thus, the definition is refined to exclude genera.

Real vs Relative Species in the Five Universals
Author distinguishes between real species and relative species and explains their relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given universal as a real species, a relative species, or both, and determine the relationship between them using the 'عموم من وجه' framework.

Core Idea
A species can be absolute or relative: real species is species in itself, relative species depends on a higher genus.

The term 'species' is not univocal in logic. The author shows that the old logicians distinguished between a species that is a species by its own essence (real species) and a species that is considered a species only in relation to a higher genus (relative species). Understanding this distinction is crucial because many terms in Islamic sciences, like 'animal' in kalam or 'act' in usul al-fiqh, function as both genus and species depending on context.

Guided Path
1
Define the two meanings of 'species'
Identify the two distinct meanings given in the passage: real species (النوع الحقيقي) which is the quiddity itself without relation, and relative species (النوع الاضافي) which is a species only with respect to a higher genus.
The passage states the Greeks originally used 'species' for the reality of a thing, but logicians extended it homonymously.
2
Identify the conditions for each type
Note that real species is always universal and essential for what is under it, excluding individuals and accidental subdivisions like Roman or Negro. Relative species can be either a real species under a genus (e.g., man under animal) or a genus under another genus (e.g., animal under growing body).
3
Map the three cases of their relationship
The passage lists three possible relationships: (1) they coincide (e.g., man is both real and relative), (2) relative exists without real (e.g., animal is relative but not a real species because it has a genus), (3) real exists without relative (if the species is simple with no genus, like a point—though debated).
This is the 'general and specific in some respects' (عموم من وجه) relationship.
4
Apply to an Islamic classification
Take an example from fiqh: the category 'worship acts' (عبادات) is a relative species under 'obligations' (واجبات) but itself is a genus for prayer, fasting, etc. Classify it as real or relative using the criteria.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و اعلم ان لفظ النوع كان معناه فى الوضع الأول عند اليونانيين حقيقة الشيء وماهيته والمنطقيون وجدوا ماهيات للاشياء التي تحت الجنس فنقلوه اليها فاطلقوه بالاشتراك اللفظى على معنيين مختلفين يقال لاحدهما: النوع الحقيقي وللاخر النوع الاضافي. ثم اعلم ان ديدن القدماء تقديم الجنس على النوع لتقدمه ذهناً و خارجاً فان الفصول ينضم الى الاجناس فيحصل الانواع فلذا قدمه المصنف تبعاً لهم. واما المتأخرون فهم يقدمون النوع لشرفه. كليا لا جزئياً ذاتياً لما تحته لا عرضياً فالشخص و الصنف كالرومى و الزنجى مثلاً خارجان عنها. والنوع الاضافى دائماً يكون اما نوعاً حقيقياً مندرجاً تحت جنس كالانسان تحت الحيوان واما جنساً مندرجاً تحت جنس آخر كالحيوان تحت الجسم النامى ففى الأول يتصادق النوع الحقيقى والاضافي و في الثاني يوجد الاضافي بدون الحقيقى و يجوز ايضاً تحقق الحقيقى بدون الاضافى فيما اذا كان النوع بسيطاً لا جزء حتى يكون جنساً له وقد مثل بالنقطة و فيه مناقشة و بالجملة النسبة بينهما هي العموم من وجه.
And know that the word 'species' originally meant, in the first usage among the Greeks, the reality and quiddity of a thing, and the logicians found quiddities for things that are under a genus, so they transferred it to them and used it homonymously for two different meanings: one is called 'real species' and the other 'relative species'. Then know that the practice of the ancients was to give precedence to genus over species because genus precedes mentally and externally, for differentiate are joined to genera and species are produced, so the author gave it precedence following them. As for the later scholars, they give precedence to species due to its nobility. [The species] is universal, not particular; essential for what is under it, not accidental, so the individual and the type (such as Roman and Negro, for example) are excluded from it. The relative species is always either a real species subsumed under a genus, like man under animal, or a genus subsumed under another genus, like animal under growing body. In the first, the real and relative species coincide; in the second, the relative exists without the real. It is also possible for the real to exist without the relative when the species is simple, having no part that could be its genus, and it was exemplified by the point, though there is a discussion about it. In summary, the relationship between them is general and specific in some respects.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage classifies species into two types: real species (النوع الحقيقي) and relative species (النوع الاضافي). The author explains that originally 'species' meant the essence of a thing, but logicians applied it to the quiddities of things under a genus, giving it two meanings. Real species is a species in itself (e.g., man), while relative species is a species relative to a higher genus but can also be a genus to something lower (e.g., animal is relative species under growing body but genus under man). The passage also notes that the ancients preferred to define genus before species, but later scholars reverse the order due to species' nobility. Key characteristics: species is universal and essential, excluding individuals and sub-species like Roman or Negro. The relationship between real and relative species is 'general and specific in some respects' (عموم من وجه): they can coincide (e.g., man is both real and relative), the relative can exist without the real (e.g., animal is relative but not a real species because it has a genus), and the real can exist without the relative (if the species has no genus, e.g., a simple point, though there is debate).

Key Terms
النوع الحقيقي
النوع الاضافي
العموم من وجه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which statement is true about the relationship between real species (النوع الحقيقي) and relative species (النوع الاضافي)?
A) They are always identical
B) They are mutually exclusive
C) They coincide in some cases and differ in others (عموم من وجه)
D) Relative species is always broader than real species

Key Takeaway
Real species and relative species overlap partially: they coincide when a term is a species only, diverge when a term is a genus for another species, and real species can exist without relative species if the species has no generic part.

Real and Relative Species: Definitions and Relationship
Author defines the two types of species and explains their relationship as generality and specificity from one perspective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real and relative species and identify their relationship as 'generality and specificity from one perspective'.

Core Idea
A species can be both real and relative, but not always.

In this lesson, we examine the distinction between the real species (النوع الحقيقي) and the relative species (النوع الإضافي) as explained in the Hashiya. Understanding this distinction is crucial for defining any term in the five universals. The author shows that these two types are not mutually exclusive; they overlap in some cases and diverge in others. He also tackles a subtle debate about whether a simple entity like a point can have a genus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definienda
The author defines two types: real species (no higher species above it) and relative species (considered in relation to its genus). Locate the phrases that indicate these definitions in the Arabic.
2
Extract the relational logic
The relationship is 'عموم من وجه' (generality and specificity from one perspective). Find the examples where real and relative species coincide (human) and differ (animal and point).
Coincidence: human is both a real species under animal and a relative species under living body. Difference: animal is a real species but not relative when considered under living body? Actually animal is relative when under living body, but the passage says they differ in animal and point. For animal, it is real but also relative as a genus under living body; but the passage says they differ, meaning not both real and relative simultaneously? The author says: 'they coincide on human and differ in animal and point.' So animal is not both? Wait, animal is a real species under living body? No, animal is a genus under living body. So animal is not a real species when under living body; it is a relative species (as a genus). So in that case, only relative exists. And point is relative but not real. So the example shows that sometimes only one applies.
3
Understand the composition of relative species
The relative species is always either a real species under a genus (like human under animal) or a genus under another genus (like animal under living body). In the first case, real and relative coincide; in the second, only relative exists. There is also the possibility of a real species without being relative, when the species is simple with no parts.
Simple species example: the point. The author discusses whether it has a genus.
4
Analyze the point debate
Some argue the point has no genus because it has no external parts. The commentator counters that genus is a mental part, not an external one, so the point can have a mental genus. This shows that even simple entities can be defined through the five universals.
This debate illustrates the importance of distinguishing between external composition (parts in the world) and mental composition (conceptual components).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحاشية المقول (ه) عليها وعلى غيرها الجنس في جواب ماهو و يختص باسم الاضافى (ه) كالاول بالحقيقى و بينهما عموم من وجه لتصادقهما على الانسان وتفارقهما في الحيوان والنقطة. كليا لا جزئياً ذاتياً لما تحته لا عرضياً فالشخص و الصنف كالرومى و الزنجى مثلاً خارجان عنها والنوع الاضافى دائماً يكون اما نوعاً حقيقياً مندرجاً تحت جنس كالانسان تحت الحيوان واما جنساً مندرجاً تحت جنس آخر كالحيوان تحت الجسم النامى ففى الأول يتصادق النوع الحقيقى والاضافي و في الثاني يوجد الاضافي بدون الحقيقى و يجوز ايضاً تحقق الحقيقى بدون الاضافى فيما اذا كان النوع بسيطاً لا جزء حتى يكون جنساً له وقد مثل بالنقطة و فيه مناقشة و بالجملة النسبة بينهما هي العموم من وجه. قوله « والنقطة»: النقطة طرف الخط والخط طرف السطح و السطح طرف الجسم فالسطح غير منقسم في العمق والخط غير منقسم في العرض والعمق فالنقطة غير منقسم فى الطول والعرض والعمق فهى عَرَض لا يقبل القسمة فى الجهات اصلاً و اذا لم يقبل القسمة اصلا لم يكن لها جزء فلا يكون لها جنس و فيه نظر، لان هذا يدل على انه لا جزء لها في الخارج و الجنس ليس جزء خارجياً بل هو من الاجزاء العقلية فجاز ان يكون للنقطة جزء عقلى و هو جنس لها و ان لم يكن لها مضايفاً له. (محمد علی)
The marginal gloss (Hashiya): The thing said of it and of others is genus in answer to 'what is it?' and it is distinguished by the name 'relative' just as the first is called 'real'. Between them is generality and specificity from one perspective because they coincide on 'human' and differ in 'animal' and 'point'. It is a universal, not a particular, essential for what is under it, not accidental. So the person and the species like 'Roman' and 'Ethiopian', for example, are excluded from it. The relative species is always either a real species subsumed under a genus, like human under animal, or a genus subsumed under another genus, like animal under living body. In the first case, the real and relative species coincide; in the second, the relative exists without the real; and it is also possible for the real to exist without the relative when the species is simple, having no part to be its genus. An example is the point, and there is discussion about it. In summary, the relationship between them is generality and specificity from one perspective. His saying: 'and the point': The point is the end of the line, the line is the end of the surface, the surface is the end of the body. The surface is not divisible in depth, the line is not divisible in width and depth, so the point is not divisible in length, width, and depth. Thus it is an accident that does not accept division in dimensions at all. Since it does not accept division at all, it has no part, so it has no genus. There is a consideration: this indicates that it has no part externally, but the genus is not an external part; rather it is among the mental parts. So it is possible for the point to have a mental part, which is its genus, even if it has no correlative. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of species: real (haqiqi) and relative (idafi). The author begins by explaining that the term 'genus' (jins) is said of multiple things in answer to 'what is it?'. The first type is called 'real species' and the second 'relative species'. Their relationship is 'general and specific in one respect' because they coincide in some cases (like 'human', which is both a real species under 'animal' and a relative species under 'living body') and differ in others (like 'animal' which is a real species but not relative, and 'point' which is a relative species but not real). The author then clarifies that individuals and subspecies like 'Roman' or 'Ethiopian' are not species at all. Next, he elaborates that the relative species is always either a real species under a genus or a genus under a higher genus. In the first subcase, real and relative coincide; in the second, only relative exists. Conversely, a real species can exist without being relative if it is simple with no parts. The 'point' is given as an example, but there is a discussion. The author then explains: the point is defined as the end of a line, and it is indivisible in all dimensions, so it has no external parts. Some argue it thus has no genus. However, the author counters that the genus is a mental part, not an external one, so the point can have a mental genus. This shows that the relationship between real and relative species is one of generality and specificity from one perspective.

Key Terms

The Point's Intellectual Genus: Distinguishing External from Intellectual Parts
Hierarchical ordering of genera and critique of point lacking genus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the intellectual part (juz' 'aqli) of a definition and explain why a point can have a genus despite lacking physical parts.

Core Idea
The genus is an intellectual part, not a physical component.

In this passage, the author refines the common misunderstanding that the point cannot have a genus because it has no physical parts. By distinguishing between external parts and intellectual parts, he preserves the point's place in the hierarchy of genera. This illustrates a key principle: logical classification is based on conceptual analysis, not physical divisibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hierarchy of genera
The author lists body, surface, line, and point based on their divisibility in dimensions. Body is divisible in depth, surface in two dimensions, line in one, and point in none.
2
Notice the challenge to the point
Because the point is not divisible at all, one might conclude it has no part and therefore no genus. This is the claim being critiqued.
3
Find the key distinction
The author responds by distinguishing 'external part' (juz' khariji) from 'intellectual part' (juz' 'aqli). The genus falls into the latter category.
4
Apply the distinction
Thus, even though the point has no external parts, it can have an intellectual part that serves as its genus. This refutes the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الكليات الخمس ثم الاجناس (ه) قد تترتب الجسم فالسطح غير منقسم في العمق والخط غير منقسم في العرض والعمق فالنقطة غير منقسم فى الطول والعرض والعمق فهى عَرَض لا يقبل القسمة فى الجهات (خ ل ) اصلاً و اذا لم يقبل القسمة اصلا لم يكن لها جزء فلا يكون لها جنس و فيه نظر، لان هذا يدل على انه لا جزء لها في الخارج و الجنس ليس جزء خارجياً (۸۳) بل هو من الاجزاء العقلية فجاز ان يكون للنقطة جزء عقلى (۸٤) و هو جنس لها و ان لم يكن لها جزء في الخارج. مضايفاً له. (محمد علی)
In the five universals, then the genera (may be ordered). Body is a surface not divisible in depth, and line is not divisible in breadth and depth, and point is not divisible in length, breadth, and depth. So it is an accident that does not accept division in directions at all. And if it does not accept division at all, it has no part, so it has no genus. And there is reflection: because this indicates that it has no part in the external, and the genus is not an external part; rather it is among the intellectual parts. So it is permissible for the point to have an intellectual part, which is its genus, even if it has no part in the external. It is added to him. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the claim that the point cannot have a genus because it has no physical parts. The author, likely Muhammad Ali (the hashiya writer), argues that the genus is not an external physical part but an intellectual concept. He uses the example of body, surface, line, and point to illustrate the hierarchy of genera: body is divisible in three dimensions, surface in two, line in one, and point in none. The point is considered an accident (عرض) that does not accept division. The objection (نظر) is that even though the point has no external part, it can have an intellectual part (جزء عقلي) which serves as its genus. This shows that the genus is a mental concept, not a physical component. The passage guards against the misunderstanding that logical genus requires physical divisibility.

The genus is an intellectual part, so even entities without physical parts can belong to a genus.

Chains of Genera and Species and the Differentia
Commentary on the chains of genera and species and the definition of differentia

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ascending and descending chains of genera and species, distinguish the differentia from other universals by the type of question it answers, and apply the concept of intermediates in hierarchical definitions.

Core Idea
The differentia answers 'what thing in its essence?' while genus and species answer 'what is it?'

Understanding how the five universals are arranged hierarchically is essential for precise definitions in Islamic sciences. This passage from a classical logic commentary clarifies the ascending chain from species to the genus of genera, and the descending chain from genus to the species of species. It also precisely defines the differentia (fasl), distinguishing it from other universals by the type of question it answers. By mastering these distinctions, you will be able to analyze definitions in fiqh, kalam, and other disciplines with greater rigor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two chains
The passage describes an ascending chain (from specific to general) and a descending chain (from general to specific). Notice the direction: ascending goes from species to genus, descending from genus to species.
The ascending chain ends at the genus of genera, the descending at the species of species.
2
Locate the endpoints
The genus of genera (jins al-ajnas) is the highest genus with no genus above it, e.g., substance. The species of species (naw' al-anwa') is the lowest species with no species under it, e.g., human.
These are the ultimate limits of the chains.
3
Understand intermediates
What lies between the highest and lowest can be intermediate genera (between genera), intermediate species (between species), or a mix (e.g., a genus between the highest genus and lowest species, or a species, or both). The commentator gives the example of the growing body as both a genus and a species.
The pronoun 'between them' is ambiguous; the commentator resolves it.
4
Analyze the differentia's definition
The differentia is defined as 'predicated of a thing in answer to 'what thing is it in its essence?'' (al-maqul fi jawab ayy shay' huwa fi dhatihi). It distinguishes the thing from others sharing the same proximate genus and is an external part of the definition.
Contrast this with genus and species, which answer 'what is it?' (ma huwa).
5
Apply to a definition
Take a definition like 'Human is a rational animal'. 'Animal' is the genus (answering 'what is it?'), and 'rational' is the differentia (answering 'what thing in its essence?'). Verify that this definition distinguishes humans from all other animals.
This shows how the differentia completes the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
